The Second Premise Defined and the Self-Evident Proof
Razi states the second premise and the self-evident proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second premise of Razi's cosmological argument and the scholarly opinion that it is a self-evident truth.

Core Idea
The need for a preponderator is a primary intuition of reason.

Razi begins by stating the crucial second premise: a possible being equally related to existence and non-existence cannot become preponderant without an external cause. He then presents the first of two scholarly views on how to establish this premise—the view that it is self-evident (badīhiyya). This lesson introduces that premise and the intuitive reasoning that supports it.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage that defines the second premise and the first opinion (self-evident). Notice the key terms: الممكن المتساوي, مرجح, مقدمة أولية بدهية.
The passage uses a conditional structure: 'لا يترجح إلا لمرجح'.
2
Understand the second premise
The second premise says that a possible being (one that equally can exist or not exist) does not have one side become preponderant over the other except by a preponderator (an external cause). This is the foundation for proving God's existence.
This premise is not just about physical events but about any possible thing.
3
Examine the self-evident argument
The first opinion holds that this premise is self-evident: reason directly sees that if two sides are equal, preponderance cannot happen without an external factor. Everyday experience—people seeking causes for events—confirms this.
4
Connect to the broader argument
Recognize that this premise is a key step in proving a Necessary Being: if every possible needs an external cause, the chain of causes must terminate in a self-sufficient cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية : قولنا : الممكن المتساوي لا يترجح أحد طرفيه على الآخر إلا لمرجح
وللعلماء في تقرير هذه المقدمة قولان:
أحدهما : أنها مقدمة أولية ،بدهية وذلك لأنه لما كانت نسبة الوجود ونسبة
العدم إلى تلك الماهية على السوية قضى صريح العقل بأنه يمتنع رجحان تأحدهما على الآخر لا لأجل انضياف أمر من الأمور إلى الطرف الراجح، ولذلك فإن كل عاقل إذا أحس بحدوث شيء في وقت معين طلب لحدوثه علة، وسبباً، وذلك يدل على أن افتقار رجحان الجائز إلى المرجح أمر مقرر في بدائه العقول.
القول الثاني : أن هذه المقدمة برهانية، وأحسن ما قيل في هذا المقام أن الاستواء والرجحان متناقضان متنافيان ولما ثبت أن ماهية الممكن مقتضية للاستواء، فلو حصل الرجحان أيضًا لاجتمع النقيضان، وهو محال.
وعندي أن هذه الحجة ضعيفة لأن التناقض إنما يلزم لو كانت الماهية مقتضية للاستواء أو مقتضية للرجحان أيضاً، أما من يقول الماهية مقتضية للاستواء وأم الرجحان فإنه حصل لا لعلة ،البتة لا لذلته ولا لغيره لم يلزم التناقض على هذا القول، والأقوى أن يقال : الممكن هو الذي يكون العدم والوجود بالنسبة إليه على التساوي والشيء الذي يكون كذلك امتنع أن يدخل في الوجود إلا أن يصير وجوده راجحًا على عدمه، وذلك الرجحان يجب أن يكون صفة الشيء آخر سابق على وجوده، فيمتنع أن يكون محل ذلك الرجحان هو وجوده؛ لأن ذلك الرجحان لو كان صفة لوجوده لكان متأخرًا عن وجوده لكنا قد بينا أنه متقدم على وجوده، فيحصل الدور، وهو محال، فإذن ذلك الرجحان يجب أن يكون صفة لشيء آخر يلزم من وجوده، وذلك هو المؤثر، فثبت أن كل ممكن فهو مفتقر إلى المؤثر.
Second premise: our statement: The equal possible (al-mumkin al-mutawāzī) does not have one of its two sides preponderant over the other except by a preponderator. The scholars have two sayings in establishing this premise: One of them: that it is a primary, self-evident premise—because since the relation of existence and non-existence to that quiddity is equal, the clear reason judges that it is impossible for one of them to be preponderant over the other without the attachment of some matter to the preponderant side; therefore, every rational person, when he perceives the occurrence of something at a specific time, seeks for its occurrence a cause and a reason, and that indicates that the dependency of the preponderance of the permissible (al-jāʼiz) upon the preponderator is a settled matter in the primary intuitions of minds. The second saying: that this premise is demonstrative (burhāniyya), and the best what has been said in this regard is that equality and preponderance are contradictory and mutually exclusive; and since it is established that the quiddity of the possible necessitates equality, if preponderance also occurred, the two contradictories would gather, which is impossible. But I (Razi) consider this argument weak because the contradiction would only follow if the quiddity necessitates equality or also necessitates preponderance. As for one who says the quiddity necessitates equality but preponderance occurs without any cause at all, not by itself nor by another, the contradiction does not follow on this view. The stronger is to say: The possible is that for which non-existence and existence are equal; a thing that is so is impossible to enter into existence unless its existence becomes preponderant over its non-existence; that preponderance must be a quality of another thing prior to its existence; therefore it is impossible that the locus of that preponderance be its own existence—because if that preponderance were a quality of its existence, it would be posterior to its existence, but we have shown it to be prior to its existence, so circularity results, which is impossible. Therefore, that preponderance must be a quality of another thing whose existence follows from it, and that is the effecter (al-muʼaththir). So it is established that every possible is in need of the effecter.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines the second premise of the cosmological proof and discusses two ways to establish it. The second premise states that a possible being (one equally disposed to existence and non-existence) cannot have one side preponderant over the other without a preponderator. The first opinion holds this premise is self-evident (badīhiyya): reason directly sees that equality cannot tip without an external factor; everyday experience of seeking causes for events confirms it. The second opinion attempts a demonstrative proof: equality and preponderance are contradictory, so if the quiddity necessitates equality, preponderance cannot occur without contradiction. Imam Razi criticizes this second proof: the contradiction only arises if the quiddity itself necessitates equality inherently; but if the quiddity merely has equality as a passive state while preponderance can come from an external cause without contradiction, the argument fails. Razi then offers his stronger argument: the possible's equality means its existence cannot occur unless a preponderance arises. That preponderance must be a quality of something other than the possible itself and must be prior to the possible's existence; otherwise circularity ensues (if it were a quality of the possible's own existence, it would be both prior and posterior). Therefore, the preponderator must be an external effecter. This establishes that every possible needs an external cause, a key step in the proof for a Necessary Being.

Key Terms
الممكن المتساوي
مرجح
مقدمة أولية بديهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the second premise is a conclusion of a prior argument, not self-evident.
Correction: The first opinion argues it is self-evident (badīhiyya), meaning it is directly known by reason without proof, as Razi explains by the universal human tendency to seek causes.

Checkpoint
According to the first opinion in the passage, why is the second premise considered self-evident?
A) Because it is proven by logical deduction.
B) Because reason immediately judges that equality cannot tip without an external cause.
C) Because it is a revealed truth from scripture.
D) Because it is based on empirical observation alone.

Key Takeaway
The second premise—that a possible being needs an external cause to become preponderant—is established as a self-evident truth according to the first opinion.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see something happen in your life—good or bad—do you naturally seek its cause? Have you ever taken that instinct to its ultimate conclusion, the Creator? What would change if you did?
?
The passage says the need for a preponderator is a settled matter in 'primary intuitions of minds.' But do you live as if that is true? When you ignore the cause behind events, are you suppressing your own reason? Ask yourself: what am I afraid of seeing?
?
If the need for a cause is truly self-evident, then why do you sometimes act as if things happen by mere chance? Commit to catching yourself the next time you assume randomness, and instead pause to ask: 'Who or what is the preponderator here?'

The Demonstrative Proof and Razi's Critique
Razi presents and critiques the demonstrative proof for the second premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second opinion that attempts a demonstrative proof for the second premise, and Imam Razi's critique showing its weakness.

Core Idea
A flawed proof is worse than no proof—Razi shows the demonstrative attempt fails.

Some scholars sought a formal demonstration (burhān) for the second premise, arguing that equality and preponderance are contradictory. Imam Razi, however, points out a subtle flaw: the contradiction only follows if the quiddity itself necessitates equality, which is not necessarily the case. This lesson examines that proof and Razi's incisive critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage containing the second opinion and Razi's critique. Identify the key claim: 'equality and preponderance are contradictory'.
The phrase 'متناقضان متنافيان' emphasizes mutual exclusion.
2
Understand the demonstrative argument
The second opinion argues: since the quiddity of the possible necessitates equality, if preponderance also occurs, the two contradictories (equality and preponderance) would coexist, which is impossible. So preponderance cannot happen without a cause that removes the equality.
3
Grasp Razi's critique
Razi says this proof only works if the quiddity itself necessitates equality in an active sense. But if the quiddity merely has equality as a passive state, preponderance could come from an external cause without logical contradiction, so the proof fails.
Razi is careful: he does not reject the premise itself, only this particular proof.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Consider why it is important to have sound arguments even for seemingly obvious truths. Razi's critique shows the need for rigorous reasoning in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

وللعلماء في تقرير هذه المقدمة قولان:
أحدهما : أنها مقدمة أولية ،بدهية وذلك لأنه لما كانت نسبة الوجود ونسبة

Second premise: our statement: The equal possible (al-mumkin al-mutawāzī) does not have one of its two sides preponderant over the other except by a preponderator. The scholars have two sayings in establishing this premise: One of them: that it is a primary, self-evident premise—because since the relation of existence and non-existence to that quiddity is equal, the clear reason judges that it is impossible for one of them to be preponderant over the other without the attachment of some matter to the preponderant side; therefore, every rational person, when he perceives the occurrence of something at a specific time, seeks for its occurrence a cause and a reason, and that indicates that the dependency of the preponderance of the permissible (al-jāʼiz) upon the preponderator is a settled matter in the primary intuitions of minds. The stronger is to say: The possible is that for which non-existence and existence are equal; a thing that is so is impossible to enter into existence unless its existence becomes preponderant over its non-existence; that preponderance must be a quality of another thing prior to its existence; therefore it is impossible that the locus of that preponderance be its own existence—because if that preponderance were a quality of its existence, it would be posterior to its existence, but we have shown it to be prior to its existence, so circularity results, which is impossible. Therefore, that preponderance must be a quality of another thing whose existence follows from it, and that is the effecter (al-muʼaththir). So it is established that every possible is in need of the effecter.

Imam Razi criticizes this second proof: the contradiction only arises if the quiddity itself necessitates equality inherently; but if the quiddity merely has equality as a passive state while preponderance can come from an external cause without contradiction, the argument fails. Razi then offers his stronger argument: the possible's equality means its existence cannot occur unless a preponderance arises. That preponderance must be a quality of something other than the possible itself and must be prior to the possible's existence; otherwise circularity ensues (if it were a quality of the possible's own existence, it would be both prior and posterior). Therefore, the preponderator must be an external effecter. This establishes that every possible needs an external cause, a key step in the proof for a Necessary Being.

Key Terms
برهانية
استواء
رجحان

A) It relies on an infinite regress.
B) It only works if the quiddity necessitates equality in an active sense, but if equality is merely passive, preponderance can come from an external cause without contradiction.
C) It confuses equality with preponderance.
D) It assumes the possible is self-caused.

Key Takeaway
Razi critiques the demonstrative proof for the second premise, showing it is not logically necessary under all interpretations of the quiddity's relation to equality.

The quiddity's necessity of equality is ambiguous. Are you certain that your understanding of Allah's attributes is built on unambiguous, sound reasoning? Razi's Stronger Argument for the Second Premise
Razi presents his own stronger argument for the second premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand and appreciate Imam Razi's own stronger argument for the second premise, which avoids circularity and establishes the need for an external effecter.

Core Idea
The preponderator must be prior to existence, so it cannot be the existence itself.

After showing the weakness of the demonstrative proof, Razi presents his own argument, which he considers stronger. This argument carefully analyzes the temporal priority of the preponderator over the possible's existence, thereby avoiding circularity and demonstrating that the preponderator must be an external cause. This lesson walks through that elegant reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read Razi's stronger argument starting from 'والأقوى أن يقال'. Follow the logical steps carefully.
Notice the conditional structure: 'الممكن هو الذي... امتنع أن يدخل في الوجود إلا أن...'
2
Understand the equality of possibility
Razi defines the possible as that for which existence and non-existence are equal. Therefore, it cannot enter existence unless its existence becomes preponderant over non-existence.
3
Analyze the priority requirement
That preponderance must be a quality of something else and must be prior to the possible's existence—otherwise circularity arises. Razi shows that if the preponderance were a quality of the possible's own existence, it would be both prior and posterior, which is impossible.
4
Conclude the need for an effecter
Therefore, the preponderance must be a quality of a separate thing, and that thing is the effecter (muʼaththir). Thus every possible needs an effecter.
5
Reflect on the strength
This argument does not rely on the ambiguous notion of quiddity necessitating equality; it uses a clear logical requirement of priority to avoid circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية : قولنا : الممكن المتساوي لا يترجح أحد طرفيه على الآخر إلا لمرجح
وللعلماء في تقرير هذه المقدمة قولان:
أحدهما : أنها مقدمة أولية ،بدهية وذلك لأنه لما كانت نسبة الوجود ونسبة
العدم إلى تلك الماهية على السوية قضى صريح العقل بأنه يمتنع رجحان تأحدهما على الآخر لا لأجل انضياف أمر من الأمور إلى الطرف الراجح، ولذلك فإن كل عاقل إذا أحس بحدوث شيء في وقت معين طلب لحدوثه علة، وسبباً، وذلك يدل على أن افتقار رجحان الجائز إلى المرجح أمر مقرر في بدائه العقول.
القول الثاني : أن هذه المقدمة برهانية، وأحسن ما قيل في هذا المقام أن الاستواء والرجحان متناقضان متنافيان ولما ثبت أن ماهية الممكن مقتضية للاستواء، فلو حصل الرجحان أيضًا لاجتمع النقيضان، وهو محال.
وعندي أن هذه الحجة ضعيفة لأن التناقض إنما يلزم لو كانت الماهية مقتضية للاستواء أو مقتضية للرجحان أيضاً، أما من يقول الماهية مقتضية للاستواء وأم الرجحان فإنه حصل لا لعلة ،البتة لا لذلته ولا لغيره لم يلزم التناقض على هذا القول، والأقوى أن يقال : الممكن هو الذي يكون العدم والوجود بالنسبة إليه على التساوي والشيء الذي يكون كذلك امتنع أن يدخل في الوجود إلا أن يصير وجوده راجحًا على عدمه، وذلك الرجحان يجب أن يكون صفة الشيء آخر سابق على وجوده، فيمتنع أن يكون محل ذلك الرجحان هو وجوده؛ لأن ذلك الرجحان لو كان صفة لوجوده لكان متأخرًا عن وجوده لكنا قد بينا أنه متقدم على وجوده، فيحصل الدور، وهو محال، فإذن ذلك الرجحان يجب أن يكون صفة لشيء آخر يلزم من وجوده، وذلك هو المؤثر، فثبت أن كل ممكن فهو مفتقر إلى المؤثر.
Second premise: our statement: The equal possible (al-mumkin al-mutawāzī) does not have one of its two sides preponderant over the other except by a preponderator. The scholars have two sayings in establishing this premise: One of them: that it is a primary, self-evident premise—because since the relation of existence and non-existence to that quiddity is equal, the clear reason judges that it is impossible for one of them to be preponderant over the other without the attachment of some matter to the preponderant side; therefore, every rational person, when he perceives the occurrence of something at a specific time, seeks for its occurrence a cause and a reason, and that indicates that the dependency of the preponderance of the permissible (al-jāʼiz) upon the preponderator is a settled matter in the primary intuitions of minds. The second saying: that this premise is demonstrative (burhāniyya), and the best what has been said in this regard is that equality and preponderance are contradictory and mutually exclusive; and since it is established that the quiddity of the possible necessitates equality, if preponderance also occurred, the two contradictories would gather, which is impossible. But I (Razi) consider this argument weak because the contradiction would only follow if the quiddity necessitates equality or also necessitates preponderance. As for one who says the quiddity necessitates equality but preponderance occurs without any cause at all, not by itself nor by another, the contradiction does not follow on this view. The stronger is to say: The possible is that for which non-existence and existence are equal; a thing that is so is impossible to enter into existence unless its existence becomes preponderant over its non-existence; that preponderance must be a quality of another thing prior to its existence; therefore it is impossible that the locus of that preponderance be its own existence—because if that preponderance were a quality of its existence, it would be posterior to its existence, but we have shown it to be prior to its existence, so circularity results, which is impossible. Therefore, that preponderance must be a quality of another thing whose existence follows from it, and that is the effecter (al-muʼaththir). So it is established that every possible is in need of the effecter.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage defines the second premise of the cosmological proof and discusses two ways to establish it. The second premise states that a possible being (one equally disposed to existence and non-existence) cannot have one side preponderant over the other without a preponderator. The first opinion holds this premise is self-evident (badīhiyya): reason directly sees that equality cannot tip without an external factor; everyday experience of seeking causes for events confirms it. The second opinion attempts a demonstrative proof: equality and preponderance are contradictory, so if the quiddity necessitates equality, preponderance cannot occur without contradiction. Imam Razi criticizes this second proof: the contradiction only arises if the quiddity itself necessitates equality inherently; but if the quiddity merely has equality as a passive state while preponderance can come from an external cause without contradiction, the argument fails. Razi then offers his stronger argument: the possible's equality means its existence cannot occur unless a preponderance arises. That preponderance must be a quality of something other than the possible itself and must be prior to the possible's existence; otherwise circularity ensues (if it were a quality of the possible's own existence, it would be both prior and posterior). Therefore, the preponderator must be an external effecter. This establishes that every possible needs an external cause, a key step in the proof for a Necessary Being.

دور
الممكن

Checkpoint

A) Because existence is eternal.

C) Because it would create circularity: the preponderance would be both prior and posterior to existence.
D) Because existence is self-sufficient.

Key Takeaway
Razi's stronger argument uses priority logic to prove that the preponderator for any possible being must be an external effecter, avoiding circularity.

The Contingency of Time as Proof for the Necessary Existent
Razi's proof for Necessary Existent from time's contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi proves that time is contingent and requires a Necessary Existent as its cause.

Core Idea
Time is not self-sufficient; its existence demands a cause beyond itself.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents a philosophical argument for the existence of God based on the nature of time. He considers three possible ways time could exist and shows that only one is rationally sound. This lesson walks through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once slowly, then read the English translation. Notice the logical structure of three possibilities.
Razi uses a very concise style; pay attention to the phrases 'لذاته', 'لغيره', and 'اتفاقي'.
2
Identify the three possibilities
The passage divides the explanation of time's existence into three categories: due to itself, due to another, or accidental. Find each one in the text.
3
Understand why two are invalid
Follow Razi's arguments for why time cannot be self-caused (would be eternal) and cannot be accidental (non-existence would be possible, but it's impossible for time not to exist).
This part requires careful reasoning; take notes if needed.
4
Grasp the conclusion
The only remaining possibility is that time is contingent in itself but necessary due to its cause. This cause is the Necessary Existent.
Razi notes that this argument from time is even stronger than from other contingent things.
5
Reflect on the hadith
Consider the Prophetic saying 'Do not revile time, for Allah is time.' Razi interprets this as time pointing to God, not being God Himself.
This shows how philosophical reasoning aligns with sacred texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان لغيره ثبت افتقاره إلى المؤثر، وإن كان لذاته وجب أن يكون منقضياً، بل يكون باقيًا مستمرا، وهو محال وإن كان لا لذاته ولا لغيره كان حدوثه اتفاقياً، وكل ما كان اتفاقيًا لم يمتنع في العقل أن لا يوجد، ولكنا قد بينا أن العدم على الزمان محال، ولما بطل القسمان ثبت القسم الأول وهو أنه ممكن لذاته إلا أنه واجب الوجود لسببه ومؤثره، فلاجل وجوبه لوجوب سببه يمتنع العدم عليه. وعند هذا يظهر أن دلالة وجود الزمان على وجود واجب الوجود، أظهر من دلالة جميع الممكنات ولعله هو المراد من قوله : لا تسبوا الدهر فإن الله الدهر، هذا ما يمكن تقريره بناء على أصول القوم.
If it [the existence of time] is due to another, its dependence on the cause is established; and if it is due to itself, it must be perishable, rather it would be enduring and continuous, which is impossible. And if it is neither due to itself nor due to another, its occurrence would be accidental, and whatever is accidental is not precluded by reason from not existing. But we have already shown that non-existence with respect to time is impossible. Since the two divisions are invalid, the first division is established: that it is possible in itself, but necessary of existence due to its cause and agent. Therefore, due to its necessity because of the necessity of its cause, non-existence is impossible for it. And upon this it becomes apparent that the evidence of the existence of time for the existence of the Necessary Existent is more manifest than the evidence of all contingent things. Perhaps this is the intended meaning of his saying: 'Do not revile time, for verily Allah is time.' This is what can be established based on the principles of the [theological] school.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of the Necessary Existent (واجب الوجود) from the contingency of time (الزمان). Razi begins by dividing the possible explanations for the existence of time into three categories: (1) its existence is due to another (غيره), meaning it depends on an external cause; (2) its existence is due to itself (لذاته); or (3) its existence is neither due to itself nor due to another, i.e., purely accidental (اتفاقي). He then argues that the second and third are impossible. If time were necessary by itself (لذاته), it would be eternal and imperishable (باقيًا مستمرا), but time is observed to be perishable, so this is false. If its existence were purely accidental, reason would allow that it might not have existed, but earlier (Razi has already established) that non-existence of time is impossible (العدم على الزمان محال). Therefore, only the first possibility remains: time is contingent (ممكن) in itself but necessitated by an external cause. This cause is the Necessary Existent. Razi then notes that this argument from time is even stronger than the usual arguments from other contingent things, and he connects it to the prophetic hadith, 'Do not revile time, for verily Allah is time,' interpreting it as an indication that time points to God. The passage ends by noting that this presentation is consistent with the principles of the theological school (أصول القوم).

Key Terms
ممكن
واجب الوجود
زمان
مؤثر
افتقار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith 'Do not revile time, for Allah is time' means time is God.
Correction: Razi interprets it as time being a sign of God, not identical to God.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's argument, what is the status of time (zaman) in relation to contingency?
A) Time is necessary by itself.
B) Time is impossible.
C) Time is contingent and requires a cause.
D) Time is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument from time provides a clear proof that a Necessary Existent must exist as the cause of time's existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

You rely on time every day—for prayer times, deadlines, your schedule—but have you ever stopped to ask yourself why time exists at all? If time itself is contingent, what does that mean for your sense of urgency and control over your own life? Will you today pause and thank the One who made time, rather than feel anxious about it?

Razi says the proof from time is clearer than other proofs. Do you see time as a neutral backdrop, or as a constant reminder that you are not self-sufficient? When you feel pressed for time, do you turn to the Creator of time, or do you simply feel stressed? Commit to one moment today where you consciously remember that your time is in Allah's hands.

The hadith warns against reviling time. When something goes wrong—a delay, a missed opportunity—do you silently blame time or fate, or do you recognize that time is a created thing that points to a Wise Creator? What will you do the next time you are tempted to complain about time?

Negation of Circularity (Dawr)
Imam Razi defines dawr and refutes it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of circularity (dawr) and the rational proof of its impossibility as presented by Imam Razi.

Core Idea
Circularity in causality is logically impossible.

Imam Razi begins this third premise by defining what circularity (dawr) means in the context of proving the Necessary Existence. He then presents a powerful argument showing why such a circular causal chain cannot exist. This lesson will unpack the definition and walk through the proof step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation to understand the flow.
2
Understand the definition of dawr
Identify the two conditions: two contingent existents, each causing the other.
3
Walk through the proof
Follow the logical steps: cause prior to effect, mutual causality leads to priority contradiction.
The proof uses the principle that priority is transitive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة : من مقدمات برهان إثبات واجب الوجود نفي الدور والدور : هو أن يحصل موجودان ممكنان ويكون كل واحد منهما علة لوجود الآخر. ومن الناس من احتج على فساد ذلك بأن قال المؤثر متقدم على الأثر بالذات، فلذا كان كل واحد منهما مؤثرًا في الآخر لكان كل واحد منهما متقدمًا بالذات على الآخر، فكان كل واحد منهما متقدما على المتقدم على نفسه، والمتقدم على المتقدم على الشيء متقدم على ذلك الشيء، فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما متقدما على نفسه بمرتبتين، وذلك محال؛ لأنه من حيث إنه متقدم يقتضي أن يكون موجودا
The third premise: from the premises of the proof for establishing the Necessary of Existence is the negation of circularity (dawr). And circularity is that two contingent existents occur and each of them is the cause of the existence of the other. And some people argued for its impossibility by saying: The influencer is prior to the effect in essence. Therefore, if each of them is an influencer on the other, then each of them would be essentially prior to the other. Then each of them would be prior to that which is prior to itself. And that which is prior to that which is prior to a thing is prior to that thing. Thus it follows that each of them is prior to itself by two degrees, and that is impossible, because insofar as it is prior, it requires that it exists [?]
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage introduces the third premise in the proof for the Necessary of Existence: the impossibility of circularity (dawr). Razi defines dawr as two contingent existents where each is the cause of the other's existence. He then presents a standard argument against it: the cause (mu'aththir) is essentially prior to the effect (athar). If each is cause of the other, each is prior to the other, leading to each being prior to that which is prior to itself. Applying the principle that what is prior to something prior to a thing is also prior to that thing, each becomes prior to itself by two degrees. Self-priority is impossible because priority implies existence, and a thing cannot exist before itself. The argument is cut off at the page break but the core conclusion is clear.

Key Terms
دور
مؤثر
أثر
متقدم بالذات
موجودان ممكنان
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument denies any causal relationships.
Correction: The argument does not deny causality in general; it specifically denies that two things can be mutual causes simultaneously.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of circularity (dawr) according to Imam Razi in this passage?
A) Two contingent existents causing each other's existence.
B) An infinite regress of causes.
C) A cause being prior to its effect.
D) The impossibility of self-causation.

Key Takeaway
Circular causality (dawr) is impossible because it leads to the logical contradiction of a thing being prior to itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How does the impossibility of dawr challenge you to break that cycle and turn to the Self-Sufficient Cause? What is one specific step you can take today to do that?

Your heart naturally seeks security. Have you ever relied on a created thing while also relying on your own actions to maintain that reliance? Reflect on how Razi's argument exposes this as a form of circular dependence. Where can you begin to redirect your trust solely to Allah?
?
Razi's proof shows that mutual dependence is logically absurd. In your worship, do you sometimes treat your actions as a cause for Allah's mercy while also expecting that mercy to enable your actions? How does this lesson help you resolve that tension, and what commitment can you make to rely on Allah alone?

Definition of Circularity and the Priority Argument
Razi defines circularity and presents a common argument against it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of circularity in causal reasoning and the classical argument against it based on essential priority.

Core Idea
Circularity is when two things cause each other—but critics claim it leads to a logical contradiction.

Imam Razi begins the third premise of his proof for the Necessary Existent by defining circularity (الدور). He then presents an argument some philosophers use to prove its impossibility. This argument relies on the notion that an agent must be essentially prior to its effect, leading to an impossible regress. Follow along as Razi sets up the problem before offering his critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of circularity
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on how Razi defines circularity: two possible existents each serving as a cause for the other.
The definition is concise and foundational.
2
Examine the priority argument
Identify the steps of the argument: the agent is essentially prior to the effect, leading to a regress of priorities and a contradiction of existence and non-existence.
Notice the logical structure: if each is prior to the other, each is prior to itself.
3
Recognize the conclusion
The argument concludes that circularity is impossible because it entails a contradiction. Understand why the proponents think this is a valid refutation.
Razi will later critique this argument, so keep it in mind.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة : من مقدمات برهان إثبات واجب الوجود نفي الدور والدور : هو أن يحصل موجودان ممكنان ويكون كل واحد منهما علة لوجود الآخر. ومن الناس من احتج على فساد ذلك بأن قال المؤثر متقدم على الأثر بالذات، فلذا كان كل واحد منهما مؤثرًا في الآخر لكان كل واحد منهما متقدما بالذات على الآخر، فكان كل واحد منهما متقدما على المتقدم على نفسه، والمتقدم على المتقدم على الشيء متقدم على ذلك الشيء، فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما متقدما على نفسه بمرتبتين، وذلك محال ؛ لأنه من حيث إنه متقدم يقتضي أن يكون موجودًا قبل، ومن حيث إنه متأخر يقتضي أن لا يكون موجودًا قبل، وهذا يقتضي أن يكون موجودًا قبل، وأن لا يكون موجودًا قبل فيلزم اجتماع العدم والوجود في الشيء الواحد من الوجه الواحد، وهو محال. واعلم أن هذه الحجة مبنية على مقدمة مشكلة وهي إثبات التقدم الذاتي، فنقول : ليس المراد من هذا التقدم بالزمان؛ لأنا قد بينا في أول هذا الكتاب أن تقدم المؤثر على الأثر لا يجب أن يكون بالزمان؛ لأن حركة الإصبع علة لحركة الخاتم مع أن هاتين الحركتين يحصلان معاً، ولا تقدم لأحدهما على الأخر بالزمان البتة. وإذا بطل القول بأن المؤثر يجب تقدمه على الأثر بالزمان فنقول : إن عنيت يقولك : المؤثر متقدم على الأثر بالذات كون المؤثر مؤثرًا في الأثر رجع حاصل هذا التقدم إلى التأثير، فقولك : لو كان كل واحد منهما مؤثرًا في الأخر لكان كل واحد منهما متقدما على الآخر مقدمة باطلة؛ لأن تاليها عين مقدمتها من غير فرق، وليس فيها إلا تبديل عبارة بعبارة، فإن كان القول بكون كل واحد منهما مؤثرًا في الآخر معلوم البطلان بالضرورة لم يكن في ذكر هذا الكلام فائدة، ولا إليه حاجة، وإن لم يكن معلوم البطلان بالضرورة لم يكن تغير العبارة مفيدا البتة. وأما إن كان المراد من قوله : المؤثر متقدم بالذات على الأثر أمرًا آخر مغايرا للقدم بالزمان ومغايرا لنفس هذا التأثير فذلك التقدم لا بد من إفادة تصوره، ثم من إقامة الدليل على ثبوته فإن كل واحد من المقامين مما غفلوا عن تلخيصه وتحصيله إلى الآن.
The third premise: from the premises of the proof of establishing the Necessary Existent: the negation of circularity. Circularity is that two possible existents occur and each of them is a cause of the existence of the other. Some people argued for the absurdity of that by saying: the agent is prior in essence (muttaqaddim bi al-dhat) to the effect, and if each of them were an agent in the other, then each of them would be essentially prior to the other, and each of them would be prior to that which is prior to itself, and that which is prior to that which is prior to a thing is prior to that thing, so it follows that each of them is prior to itself by two degrees, and that is impossible. Because inasmuch as it is prior, it requires that it exists before; and inasmuch as it is posterior, it requires that it does not exist before; and this requires that it exists before and does not exist before; so it entails the conjunction of non-existence and existence in one thing from one aspect, and that is impossible. Know that this argument is based on a problematic premise, namely the establishment of essential priority (al-taqaddum al-dhati). We say: it is not meant by this priority temporal priority; for we have shown at the beginning of this book that the priority of the agent to the effect is not necessarily temporal, because the movement of the finger is a cause of the movement of the ring while these two movements occur together, and there is no temporal priority of one over the other at all. And if the statement that the agent must be prior in time to the effect is invalidated, we say: if by your saying 'the agent is essentially prior to the effect' you mean that the agent is an agent in the effect, then the reality of this priority reduces to effecting (al-ta'thir), so your saying 'if each of them were an agent in the other, then each would be prior to the other' is a false premise, because its consequent is identical to its antecedent with no difference, and it is merely a substitution of expression for expression. If the statement that each of them is an agent in the other is known to be absurd necessarily, then there is no point in mentioning this speech and no need for it; and if it is not known to be absurd necessarily, then changing the expression is not beneficial at all. And if what is meant by his saying 'the agent is essentially prior to the effect' is something else, different from temporal priority and different from this effecting itself, then that priority must be conceptualized and then proof established for its existence; for each of the two positions they have neglected to summarize and accomplish until now.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage is a definition followed by a critique of a flawed argument against circularity. Razi first defines circularity (الدور) as the situation where two possible existents each serve as the cause of the other's existence. He then presents an argument that some people use to prove its impossibility: the agent must be essentially prior to the effect, leading to a regress of priorities and a contradiction of existence and non-existence. Razi then critically examines this argument, pointing out that essential priority is problematic. He argues that if 'essential priority' means temporal priority, it is false because causes and effects can be simultaneous (e.g., finger movement and ring movement). If it means simply being an agent, then the argument becomes circular: it merely restates that each is an agent in the other without proving impossibility. If 'essential priority' means something else entirely, then it must first be properly defined and proven, which the proponents have failed to do. Thus, Razi demonstrates that the argument from essential priority is either redundant or unsubstantiated, and therefore not a sound refutation of circularity.

Key Terms
دور
المؤثر
الأثر
التقدم الذاتي
العلة
المعلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The priority argument is a valid proof that circularity is impossible.
Correction: Razi will later show this argument is flawed; it relies on an unclear and unproven notion of essential priority.

Checkpoint
According to Razi's definition in the passage, what is circularity?
A) One thing causing itself.
B) Two possible existents each being the cause of the other.
C) An infinite regress of causes.
D) Temporal priority of cause over effect.

Key Takeaway
Circularity is defined as two possible existents each being the cause of the other, and the priority argument attempts to refute it by deriving a contradiction from essential priority.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, are there any 'circular' patterns where you rely on something that relies on you, creating a cycle of dependency? How does this logical impossibility challenge you to break that cycle by turning to the Self-Sufficient One?
?
The argument shows that essential priority cannot be reversed without contradiction. Who or what truly has essential priority in your life—your desires, your work, or your Creator? Are you willing to reorder your priorities to reflect that truth?

Razi's Critique of the Priority Argument
Razi critiques the priority argument on three grounds.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's three-pronged critique of the essential priority argument against circularity.

Core Idea
Razi dismantles the priority argument by showing it either relies on false temporal priority, is tautological, or is unproven.

After presenting the argument against circularity based on essential priority, Imam Razi subjects it to a rigorous critique. He first clarifies that essential priority does not mean temporal priority, as demonstrated by simultaneous causation. Then he shows that if essential priority reduces to being an agent, the argument is simply a restatement of the original claim. Finally, he demands that if it means something else, it must be defined and proven. This lesson unpacks each step of Razi's incisive analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problematic premise
Razi points out that the entire argument rests on the concept of essential priority (التقدم الذاتي), which he will now scrutinize.
2
Reject temporal priority
Razi argues that essential priority is not temporal, using the example of the finger and ring moving simultaneously.
This shows that a cause can be simultaneous with its effect.
3
Analyze the meaning of essential priority
Razi examines two possible meanings: (1) If it means simply being an agent, the argument is tautological. (2) If it means something else, it is undefined and unproven.
In either case, the argument fails to prove the impossibility of circularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Refuting Circularity and the Objection on Relations
Razi refutes circularity and answers a relational objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why circular reasoning (dawr) is impossible according to Razi's proof based on dependence (iftiqar) and how he addresses the objection about correlational entities.

Core Idea
Circular dependence collapses into self-dependence, which is impossible.

Razi presents a robust proof against circularity by analyzing the concept of dependence (iftiqar). He then anticipates an objection that arises from correlational entities (al-idafiyan), which seem to involve mutual causation. In this lesson, you'll follow Razi's reasoning and see how he preserves the soundness of his proof against this challenge.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Proof Against Circularity
Read Razi's initial argument: if two things were each the effect of the other, each would depend on itself, which is impossible because dependence requires two distinct things.
Notice that Razi uses the concept of 'need' (iftiqar) as the basis.
2
Grasp the Objection on Correlational Entities
Identify the objection: someone claims that correlational entities like father and son are each a cause of the other because their existences are mutually entailed.
This is a common challenge to any argument against mutual causation.
3
Examine Razi's Two-Part Answer
Razi offers two responses depending on one's view of relations: either they have no external existence (so no real entities to cause each other), or their correlation is due to a single underlying cause.
Both responses preserve the validity of his proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأولى في إبطال الدور أن يقال : المعلول مفتقر إلى العلة، فلو كان كل واحد منهما معلولاً للآخر لكان كل واحد منهما مفتقرًا إلى الآخر، فكان كل واحد منهما مفتقرًا إلى المفتقر إلى نفسه فيلزم كون كل واحد منهما مفتقرًا إلى نفسه، وذلك محال، لأن الافتقار إلى الشيء إضافة بين المفتقر والمفتقر إليه، والإضافة لا تعقل إلا بين الشيئين. فإن قال قائل : الإضافيان كل واحد منهما علة لوجود الآخر، وذلك لأنه يلزم من فرض وجود أيهما كان وجود الآخر قطعاً، ومن فرض عدم أيهما كان عدم الآخر قطعاً، فوجب كون كل واحد منهما علة لوجود الآخر. فالجواب : إن قلنا : إن الإضافات لا وجود لها في الأعيان فقد زال السؤال، وإن قلنا : إن لها وجودًا في الأعيان قلنا : الملازمة بينهما معللة بوحدة السبب.
And the more appropriate [approach] in refuting circularity is to say: the effect is in need (muftaqir) of the cause. So if each of them were an effect of the other, then each of them would be in need of the other, and each of them would be in need of that which is in need of itself, so it follows that each of them is in need of itself, and that is impossible, because the need for a thing is a relation (idafa) between the needy and the needed, and a relation cannot be conceptualized except between two things. If someone says: the correlational entities (al-idafiyan) each are a cause of the existence of the other, because from the assumption of the existence of either one, the existence of the other necessarily follows, and from the assumption of the non-existence of either one, the non-existence of the other necessarily follows; therefore each must be a cause of the existence of the other. The answer: if we say that relations (al-idafat) have no existence in the external world (fi al-a'yan), then the question disappears; and if we say that they have existence in the external world, we say that the concomitance (al-mulazama) between them is explained by the unity of the cause (wahdat al-sabab).
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents Razi's own proof against circularity and then answers an objection. He states that the sounder way to refute circularity is based on dependence (iftaqar): the effect is in need of its cause. If two things were each the effect of the other, then each would be in need of the other, and thus each would be in need of that which is in need of itself, leading to self-dependence, which is impossible because dependence is a relation that requires two distinct things. The objection raises the case of correlational entities (al-idafiyan), where the existence of each is necessarily connected to the other, making them appear as mutual causes. Razi offers two responses depending on one's metaphysics of relations. If one holds that relations have no extra-mental existence (a position Razi favors), then there are no real entities posited to cause each other, so the objection fails. If one holds that relations do exist externally, then the mutual necessity is not due to each being the cause of the other but rather to a shared underlying cause (wahdat al-sabab) that explains the correlation. In either case, the objection does not undermine the proof, which ultimately shows that circularity leads to an impossible self-dependence.

Key Terms
مفتقر
إضافة
الإضافيان
علة
معلول
الملازمة
وحدة السبب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Correlational entities like father and son are each the cause of the other, proving mutual causation.
Correction: Razi shows either they are not real existents (so no causation) or their correlation is due to a shared cause.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the objection that correlational entities (al-idafiyan) are each a cause of the other?
A) He denies that correlational entities exist at all.
B) He argues that the concomitance is due to a single cause.
C) Both A and B are given as possible answers.
D) He says the objection is irrelevant because correlation does not imply causation.

Key Takeaway
Razi proves that circularity leads to impossible self-dependence, and correlational entities do not refute this because either they lack external reality or share a single cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just learned that self-dependence is impossible. In your own life, are there things you rely on that actually depend on you, creating a cycle of false reliance? Will you trace them back to their true source today?
?
Razi points out that correlation does not mean causation. Are there patterns in your spiritual life—like good deeds and blessings—that you mistakenly think you cause, when they actually come from a single Cause? Will you repent of that assumption?
?
The objector’s argument seems plausible but leads to a dead end. Where in your thinking about God might you be falling for a plausible but false argument? Will you submit your reasoning to the truth of this proof?

Imam Razi's First Proof Against Infinite Regress
Razi presents a proof that an infinite regress of causes leads to a Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Razi uses the impossibility of an infinite chain of causes to prove the existence of a Necessary Being.

Core Idea
An infinite chain of possibilities cannot explain itself; it requires a Necessary Being.

Imam Razi (RA) presents a rigorous logical proof that an infinite regress of causes and effects is impossible. By analyzing the nature of a hypothetical infinite series, he demonstrates that such a series would itself be contingent and thus require a cause outside itself. This argument ultimately leads to the affirmation of a Necessary Being (Wajib al-Wujud). Here, we will walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the context of the proof
Recall that Razi has established that cause and effect coexist. This is the foundational premise for his argument. Without this, the proof would not work.
The coexistence principle ensures that if causes were infinite, they would all exist now.
2
Identify the two possibilities for the totality
Razi considers the entire infinite series of causes and effects as a whole (majmu'). He asks: is this whole necessary (wajib) or possible (mumkin) in itself?
Necessary means existence is intrinsic; possible means existence depends on another.
3
Follow the reasoning that rules out necessity
The whole cannot be necessary because it depends on its parts, and each part is possible. What depends on possible things is itself possible.
This step uses the principle of dependency: if a thing depends on something possible, it is more likely to be possible.
4
Conclude that the whole requires an external cause
Since the whole is possible, it needs an efficient cause (mu'aththir) distinct from itself and its parts. This cause must be outside the entire set of possibilities.
That external cause cannot be possible because everything else is possible; it must be necessary.
5
Finalize the proof
The argument terminates: the external cause is a Necessary Being (wajib bi-dhatihi). Thus, the chain of possibilities cannot be infinite; it must lead to a First Cause.
This is the first of Razi's three proofs against infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الأول: لو تسلسلت الأسباب والمسببات إلى غير نهاية لكانت بأسرها موجودة في الحال، بناء على المقدمة التي قررناها من أن العلة والمعلول يوجدان معاً، وإذا كان كذلك فنقول: مجموع تلك الأسباب والمسببات إما أن يكون واجبًا لذاته أو ممكنا لذاته والأول باطل لأن كل مجموع فهو مفتقر إلى كل واحد من آحاده، وكل واحد من آحاد هذا المجموع ممكن لذاته، والمفتقر إلى الممكن لذاته أولى بأن يكون ممكنا لذاته، فهذا المجموع ممكن لذاته، وكل واحد من آحاده ممکن لذاته وكل ممكن لذاته فله مؤثر مغاير له، فهذا المجموع مفتقر بحسب مجموعيته، وبحسب كل واحد من آحاد مجموعيته إلى مؤثر مغاير له، وكل ما كان مغايرًا لمجموع الممكنات ولكل واحد من الممكنات لم يكن ممكنا لذاته، وكل موجود لا يكون ممكنا لذاته كان واجبًا لذاته، فثبت بهذا البرهان وجوب انتهاء الممكنات إلى موجود واجب لذاته وهو المطلوب.
The First Proof: If the causes and effects regress infinitely, then they would all exist at present, based on the preliminary we established that cause and effect exist together. If that is so, then we say: The totality of those causes and effects is either necessary by itself or possible by itself. The first is false, because every whole is dependent on each one of its parts, and each one of the parts of this whole is possible by itself, and that which is dependent on what is possible by itself is more worthy of being possible by itself. So this whole is possible by itself, and each one of its parts is possible by itself, and everything possible by itself has an efficient cause other than itself. So this whole, in terms of its totality and in terms of each one of its parts, is dependent on an efficient cause other than itself. And everything that is other than the totality of possible things and other than each one of the possible things is not possible by itself, and every existent that is not possible by itself is necessary by itself. So it is established by this proof the necessity of the termination of possible things to an existent necessary by itself, and that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage presents the first of three proofs against infinite regress (tasalsul). Razi applies the previously established principle (cause and effect coexist) to an infinite series of causes and effects. He argues that if such a series existed, all its members would exist simultaneously. He then considers the totality (majmu') of this series. This totality is either necessary (wajib) or possible (mumkin) in itself. It cannot be necessary because every whole depends on its parts, and each part is possible. Since the whole depends on what is possible, it is itself possible. As a possible entity, it needs an external cause (mu'aththir) distinct from itself and its parts. That external cause must be something other than the entire set of possible things. Whatever is other than all possible things cannot be possible itself, and therefore must be necessary in itself. Thus the proof concludes that the chain of possibilities must terminate in a Necessary Being (wajib al-wujud).

Key Terms
تسلسل
مجموع
ممكن
واجب
مؤثر مغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An infinite chain of causes could be self-sustaining without needing a first cause.
Correction: Razi shows that even an infinite chain, considered as a whole, is possible because each part is possible. As a possible entity, it requires a cause outside itself, leading to a Necessary Being.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi argue that the totality of an infinite regress cannot be necessary in itself?
A) Because it is infinite.
B) Because every whole depends on its parts, and each part is possible.
C) Because it exists in the present.
D) Because it has no beginning.

Key Takeaway
Imam Razi's first proof shows that an infinite chain of possible causes cannot be self-sufficient; it must rely on a Necessary Being outside the chain.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Razi's proof shows that the entire chain of causes in the universe, including your own existence, depends on a Necessary Being outside it. Do you live as though you are self-sufficient, or do you constantly acknowledge your contingency? This week, before each meal, pause to say: 'I am dependent on the Necessary Being for this provision.'
?
Think about a habit or comfort you rely on—something that you treat as if it were necessary for your happiness. How would your attitude change if you truly saw it as just another contingent link in the chain? Commit to detaching one small attachment today by recognizing its dependence on God.
?
This proof challenges the assumption that an infinite series can explain itself. In what area of your life do you avoid asking 'why' because the answer might reveal your own need for God? Choose one such area and honestly trace its chain of causes until you reach the Necessary Being, then offer a prayer of submission.

Second Proof: External Cause of the Possible Whole
Razi's second proof that the cause must be external.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the cause of the entire series of possible things cannot be a part of that series and must be a Necessary Being external to it.

Core Idea
The cause of the whole cannot be the whole itself or any part of it; only something external can be the cause.

Imam Razi now presents a second, more refined argument against the possibility of an infinite regress of causes. Building on the notion that the entire chain of possible things is itself possible and therefore requires a cause, he systematically eliminates the possibilities that the cause could be internal to the chain. This lesson walks through that elimination and its powerful conclusion that every chain of possible things must terminate in a Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read the Second Proof passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Focus on the logical flow: premise → three possibilities → elimination of two → conclusion.
Notice the technical terms: مجموع, مؤثر, مركب, فرد.
2
Identify the three possible causes
The argument presents three candidates for the cause of the whole: the whole itself, a part inside the whole, or something external. Understand each option.
3
Analyze why the internal part fails
Razi shows that if a part is the cause of the whole, that part would cause itself (impossible) and cause its own cause (circular). Grasp this reductio ad absurdum.
This hinges on the composite nature of the series.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثاني: لما ثبت أن ذلك المجموع ممكن لذاته، وكل ممكن لذاته فله مؤثر فلذلك المجموع مؤثر، والمؤثر في ذلك المجموع إما أن يكون هو نفس ذلك المجموع، أو شيء من الأمور الداخلة عليه أو شيء من الأمور الخارجة عنه، لا جائز أن يكون المؤثر في ذلك المجموع هو نفس ذلك المجموع لامتناع كون الشيء مؤثرًا في نفسه، ولا جائز أن يكون المؤثر فيه شيئًا من الأمور الداخلة فيه؛ لأن كل ما كان مؤثرًا في وجود مركب وجب كونه مؤثرًا في جميع أفراد ذلك المركب. وذلك الفرد الذي جعلناه علة لذلك المركب لما كان أحد أفراد ذلك المركب لزم كونه علة لنفسه ولزم كونه علة لعلة نفسه والأول باطل لامتناع كون الشيء علة لنفسه، والثاني باطل لامتناع الدور، ولما بطل أن تكون علة ذلك المجموع نفسه أو فردًا من الأفراد الداخلة فيه وجب أن تكون علته أمرًا خارجًا عنه والخارج عن مجموع الممكنات بالذات لا يكون ممكنا لذاته، وكل موجود لا يكون ممكنا لذاته وجب أن يكون واجبًا لذاته، فثبت وجوب انتهاء جميع الممكنات إلى موجود واجب لذاته، وهو المطلوب.
The Second Proof: Since it is established that that whole is possible by itself, and everything possible by itself has an efficient cause, then that whole has an efficient cause. The efficient cause of that whole is either that whole itself, or something from among the things internal to it, or something from among the things external to it. It is not possible that the efficient cause of that whole be that whole itself because of the impossibility of a thing being its own efficient cause. Nor is it possible that its efficient cause be something from among the things internal to it, because everything that is an efficient cause in the existence of a composite must be an efficient cause of all the individuals of that composite. And that individual which we made the cause of that composite, since it is one of the individuals of that composite, would necessitate it being the cause of itself and the cause of its own cause. The first is invalid because of the impossibility of a thing being the cause of itself, and the second is invalid because of the impossibility of circularity. And since it is invalid that the cause of that whole be itself or an individual from among the individuals internal to it, it must be that its cause is something external to it, and what is external to the totality of possible things by essence is not possible by itself, and every existent that is not possible by itself must be necessary by itself. So it is established the necessity of the termination of all possible things to an existent necessary by itself, and that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof against infinite regress. Imam Razi builds on the conclusion from the first proof that the totality (majmu') of the infinite series is possible (mumkin). As a possible entity, it requires a cause. He then examines three possibilities for this cause: 1) the whole itself, 2) a part internal to it, or 3) something external. He rules out the first because a thing cannot be its own cause. He rules out the second with a careful argument: if a particular part (fard) within the composite were the cause of the whole, then since that part is a constituent of the whole, it would be causing itself (as part of the whole) and also causing its own cause (because it causes the whole, which includes itself). This leads to either self-causation (impossible) or circular causation (impossible). Therefore the cause must be external. That external cause, being distinct from the entire set of possible things, cannot itself be possible, so it is necessary. Thus the proof establishes the existence of a Necessary Being.

Key Terms
مجموع
ممكن
مؤثر
مركب
فرد
علة
دور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: An individual member of an infinite series could be the cause of the whole series.
Correction: Razi shows that would lead to the individual causing itself and causing its own cause, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi reject the possibility that an individual member of the infinite series could be the cause of the whole series?
A) Because that individual would be finite and cannot cause an infinite series.
B) Because that individual would be both cause of itself and cause of its own cause, which is impossible.
C) Because that individual is not external to the series.
D) Because the series is infinite, so no individual can be its cause.

Key Takeaway
The cause of the entire chain of possible things cannot be part of that chain; it must be a Necessary Being external to all possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every possible thing in your life depends on an external Necessary Being, why do you often act as though you are self-sufficient? How would your daily choices change if you truly lived knowing that your very existence is sustained by something outside yourself?
?
Razi argues that nothing in the chain of possible things can be its own cause. Are you trying to be the cause of your own worth, identity, or purpose? What would it mean to admit that these must come from outside you?
?
The proof requires that the cause be external to the whole of possibility. Have you been looking for meaning or peace within the created world (within the 'whole')? How does this proof challenge you to seek the external, Necessary Being instead?

Third Proof Against Infinite Regress (Incomplete)
Razi begins a third proof using comparison of sums from an infinite regress, but the argument is cut off.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Imam Razi's third proof against infinite regress uses a pairing method to expose a contradiction, and how the argument begins even though it remains incomplete.

Core Idea
Razi's third proof: comparing infinite sets by pairing reveals a contradiction when the series has no beginning.

This passage introduces Imam Razi's third proof against infinite regress. He constructs two aggregates from an infinite series and attempts to pair their members. The argument is cut off mid-sentence, but the method of 'application' (tatbiq) is key. Understanding this approach will help you see how kalam theologians argued for a finite causal chain.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the literal translation. Note that the argument breaks off halfway through.
The passage ends with 'فإما أن يتقابل...'—the proof is incomplete.
2
Identify the two aggregates
Razi takes two aggregates from the infinite series: one from the last effect going back without a beginning, and another from the fifth rank of effects also going back without a beginning. The first aggregate is longer by four ranks.
The offset of four ranks is crucial for the coming contradiction.
3
Understand the pairing method
Razi describes 'application' (tatbiq): aligning the ends of the two aggregates so that the first member of one pairs with the first of the other, the second with the second, etc. Since one aggregate is longer, some members will be left unpaired.
This mismatch is intended to show a contradiction in the notion of an actual infinite.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثالث: لو تسلسلت الأسباب والمسببات لا إلى نهاية فعلى هذا التقدير الأثر الأخير موجود إلا أن الخصم يقول: المؤثر الأول غير موجود. فلنأخذ من هذا الأثر الأخير - لا إلى بداية - جملة واحدة، ولنأخذ أيضًا من المرتبة الخامسة من مراتب الآثار - لا إلى بداية - جملة أخرى فلا بد وأن تكون الجملة الأولى أزيد من الجملة الثانية بأربع مراتب ثم لنطبق هذا الطرف الأخير من جانب الآثار على الطرف الأخير من هاتين الجملتين، ومعنى هذا التطبيق أن الأول هذه الجملة مقابل بالأول من تلك الجملة، والثاني من هذه بالثاني من الجملة، فإما أن يتقابل كل واحد من آحاد الجملة الزائدة بواحد من آحاد الجملة من تلك
The Third Proof: If the causes and effects were to regress without end, then on this estimation the last effect exists, except that the opponent says that the first efficient cause does not exist. Let us take from this last effect—without a beginning—one aggregate, and let us also take from the fifth rank of the ranks of effects—without a beginning—another aggregate. Then the first aggregate must be greater than the second aggregate by four ranks. Then let us apply this last end from the side of effects to the last end of these two aggregates. The meaning of this application is that the first of this aggregate is opposite the first of that aggregate, and the second of this is opposite the second of that aggregate. Then either each one of the units of the larger aggregate corresponds to one of the units of the aggregate from that...
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage begins the third proof against infinite regress. Razi considers an infinite series of effects without a first cause. He proposes taking an aggregate (jumla) starting from the last effect and going back without a beginning, and another aggregate starting from the fifth rank of effects also going back without a beginning. The first aggregate should be longer by four ranks. Then he describes a method of 'application' (tatbiq) where the last end of each aggregate is aligned with the last end of the other, meaning the first member of one corresponds to the first of the other, the second to the second, etc. The argument aims to show a contradiction: since the first aggregate has more members, when they are paired, the larger one will have extra members that cannot be paired, implying a finite difference on an infinite series, which is problematic. However, the text breaks off before the conclusion. The completion would likely show that this leads to a contradiction, thus proving that an infinite regress is impossible.

Key Terms
تسلسل
أثر أخير
جملة
مرتبة
تطبيق
مقابلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The third proof is simply comparing numbers to show one aggregate is larger.
Correction: The proof uses the method of 'application' (tatbiq), which is a one-to-one pairing of elements from two infinite sets. The contradiction arises not from numerical comparison but from the fact that the larger aggregate has extra members that cannot be paired, implying that an actual infinite cannot exist.

Checkpoint
Why does Razi take the second aggregate from the fifth rank of effects rather than from the last effect?
A) To make the second aggregate smaller by four ranks.
B) Because the fifth rank is the middle of the series.
C) To create an offset that makes the first aggregate longer by a finite number.
D) Because the opponent believes the fifth rank is the first cause.

Key Takeaway
The third proof attempts to show that an infinite regress leads to a contradiction when comparing two aggregates offset by a finite number, though the argument is incomplete here.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Razi's proof shows that an endless chain of causes is impossible. Yet we often live as if our excuses and delays have no beginning—just an endless loop. Where in your life have you been avoiding the 'first cause' of your inaction? What step can you take now to break that chain and turn back to Allah?

The Second Proof: Contingent Composite Needs an External Cause
Imam Razi presents a proof for the necessary existent via impossibility of self-causation and circularity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the aggregate of all possible things cannot be caused by itself or any internal part, necessitating an external necessary existent.

Core Idea
Nothing causes itself, and circular causality is impossible – the universe points to a necessary cause outside it.

Imam Razi now presents a second proof for the existence of a necessary being. He starts with the aggregate of all possible things and argues that its cause cannot be itself or any of its parts. This forces the conclusion that the cause must be external to all possibles, and thus necessary in itself. Let's follow his reasoning closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without pausing. Then read the literal translation. Let the logical flow of the proof sink in.
Notice the logical structure: premise, three options, elimination of two, conclusion.
2
Identify the three possible causes
The proof lists three candidates for the cause of the aggregate: the aggregate itself, an internal part, or something external. Underline each in the text.
3
Understand why self-causation is rejected
The passage states it is 'impossible for something to effect itself.' Reflect on why this is self-evidently absurd.
This is a foundational principle in kalam.
4
Understand why an internal part fails
If a part were the cause, it would have to cause itself and cause its own cause, leading to circularity, which is also impossible. Trace this argument in the text.
Circularity (al-dawr) is a key concept.
5
Conclude with the necessary existent
Once the first two options are ruled out, the cause must be external. Anything external to all possibles cannot be possible, so it must be necessary. Confirm this conclusion in the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثاني: لما ثبت أن ذلك المجموع ممكن لذاته، وكل ممكن لذاته فله مؤثر فلذلك المجموع مؤثر، والمؤثر في ذلك المجموع إما أن يكون هو نفس ذلك المجموع، أو شيء من الأمور الداخلة عليه أو شيء من الأمور الخارجة عنه، لا جائز أن يكون المؤثر في ذلك المجموع هو نفس ذلك المجموع لامتناع كون الشيء مؤثرًا في نفسه، ولا جائز أن يكون المؤثر فيه شيئًا من الأمور الداخلة فيه؛ لأن كل ما كان مؤثرًا في وجود مركب وجب كونه مؤثرًا في جميع أفراد ذلك المركب. وذلك الفرد الذي جعلناه علة لذلك المركب لما كان أحد أفراد ذلك المركب لزم كونه علة لنفسه ولزم كونه علة لعلة نفسه والأول باطل لامتناع كون الشيء علة لنفسه، والثاني باطل لامتناع الدور، ولما بطل أن تكون علة ذلك المجموع نفسه أو فردًا من الأفراد الداخلة فيه وجب أن تكون علته أمرًا خارجًا عنه والخارج عن مجموع الممكنات بالذات لا يكون ممكنا لذاته، وكل موجود لا يكون ممكنا لذاته وجب أن يكون واجبًا لذاته، فثبت وجوب انتهاء جميع الممكنات إلى موجود واجب لذاته، وهو المطلوب.
The Second Proof: When it is established that that aggregate is possible in itself, and every possible in itself has an effecter, then that aggregate has an effecter. The effecter of that aggregate is either the aggregate itself, or something from the things internal to it, or something from the things external to it. It is not possible that the effecter of that aggregate is the aggregate itself, because it is impossible for something to effect itself. Nor is it possible that the effecter is something from the things internal to it, because everything that effects the existence of a composite must effect all the individuals of that composite. That individual which we made the cause of that composite, since it is one of the individuals of that composite, necessitates that it be a cause of itself and necessitates that it be a cause of the cause of itself. The first is false due to the impossibility of a thing being a cause of itself, and the second is false due to the impossibility of circularity. When it is false that the cause of that aggregate is itself or an individual from the individuals internal to it, it is necessary that its cause be something external to it. What is external to the aggregate of all possible things by essence is not possible in itself. And every existent that is not possible in itself must be necessary in itself. Thus it is established that all possible things must terminate in an existent necessary in itself, which is what was sought.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the existence of a necessary existent. It begins by assuming that the aggregate of all possible things is itself possible. Every possible requires an effecter (mu'aththir). The effecter of this aggregate can only be one of three: the aggregate itself, something internal to it, or something external to it. The first two are impossible. The aggregate cannot cause itself because self-causation is absurd. An internal part cannot be the cause because it would have to cause itself and cause the cause of itself, leading to circularity (al-dawr). Therefore the cause must be external to the aggregate. What is external to all possibles must be necessary in itself. Hence the necessary existent is proven.

Key Terms
البرهان الثاني
ممكن لذاته
مؤثر
مركب
علة
دور
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the 'aggregate of all possibles' is a real entity, but it is a conceptual construct; the proof works because even a conceptual aggregate requires a cause for its possibility.
Correction: The proof does not rely on the aggregate being an actual existing thing; it is a mental consideration. Every possible thing, whether considered individually or collectively, requires an external cause.

Checkpoint
In the second proof, why can the effecter of the aggregate not be one of its parts?
A) Because it would require the part to be external to the aggregate
B) Because it would lead to the part being a cause of itself and a cause of its own cause
C) Because the part is not possible in itself
D) Because the aggregate is necessary

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of self-causation and circularity forces the cause of all possible things to be an external necessary existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are part of this aggregate of possible things. If everything contingent needs a cause outside itself, what does that mean for your own existence? Do you live as if you are self-sufficient, or do you recognize your utter dependence on the Necessary Being every moment?
?
Imam Razi argues that the cause cannot be internal to the aggregate. Have you been seeking ultimate meaning, purpose, or security within yourself or within this world? Why do you still resist looking outside to the Necessary Being?
?
The proof shows that self-causation is impossible. In what areas of your life do you act as if you are the cause of your own success, wealth, or happiness? How does acknowledging your contingency and the necessity of an external cause humble you and reshape your gratitude?
