Imam Razi's Third Proof: Refuting Infinite Regress by Matching Series
Imam Razi refutes infinite regress by aligning two series of effects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Razi's proof that infinite regress of causes leads to contradiction, establishing the necessity of a first cause.

Core Idea
Infinite regress of causes collapses because whole cannot equal part.

Imam Razi presents a clever argument against the possibility of an infinite chain of causes and effects. By comparing two series of effects, he shows that if the chain were infinite, the whole would equal the part—a logical impossibility. This forces the conclusion that the series must have a beginning, leading to the Necessary Existent.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Understand the overall flow of the argument.
Notice the logical structure: assumption, construction, matching, two outcomes, conclusion.
2
Understand the setup
Assume an infinite regress of causes and effects. Take two series from the last effect: one full series and another starting from the fifth rank. The first series is longer by four terms.
The 'last effect' is the effect present in the present moment.
3
Grasp the matching process
Align the two series from the last effect backward. Match the first of one series with the first of the other, the second with the second, and so on.
This alignment is from the end (the present effect) toward the beginning.
4
Consider the two possibilities
Either every term of the longer series matches a term of the shorter series (so the whole equals the part, impossible), or the shorter series runs out, making it finite, and then the longer series is also finite.
Both possibilities lead to contradiction with the assumption of infinity.
5
Conclude the proof
Since infinite regress is impossible, the chain must have a first term. That first term must be self-sufficient (necessary in itself) to avoid an infinite regress of causes.
This establishes the existence of a Necessary Existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
البرهان الثالث : لو تسلسلت الأسباب والمسببات لا إلى نهاية فعلى هذا التقدير الأثر الأخير موجود إلا أن الخصم يقول : المؤثر الأول غير موجود. فلنأخذ من هذا الأثر الأخير - لا إلى بداية - جملة واحدة، ولنأخذ أيضًا من المرتبة الخامسة من مراتب الآثار - لا إلى بداية - جملة أخرى فلا بد وأن تكون الجملة الأولى أزيد من الجملة الثانية بأربع مراتب ثم لنطبق هذا الطرف الأخير من جانب الآثار على الطرف الأخير من هاتين الجملتين، ومعنى هذا التطبيق أن الأول هذه الجملة مقابل بالأول من تلك الجملة، والثاني من هذه بالثاني من الجملة، فإما أن يتقابل كل واحد من آحاد الجملة الزائدة بواحد من آحاد الجملة الناقصة لا إلى بداية، فيكون الكل مثل البعض، وذلك محال - وإما أن تنقطع الجملة الناقصة من الطرف الأخير، فتكون الجملة الناقصة متناهية من ذلك الطرف، والزائدة زادت عليها بأربعة فتكون الجملة الزائدة أيضًا متناهية من ذلك الطرف، فيكون للأسباب والمسببات في سلمة التصاعد طرف ومبدأ، وذلك يمنع من القول بأنه لا نهاية لها، ثم ذلك الطرف إن كان ممكنا لذاته افتقر إلى مؤثر آخر فلم يكن طرفاً، هذا خلف، وإن كان واجبًا لذاته فذلك هو المطلوب
The Third Proof: If causes and effects regressed to infinity, then on this assumption the last effect exists, but the opponent says the first effecter does not exist. So let us take from this last effect—without beginning—one series, and also take from the fifth rank of the ranks of effects—without beginning—another series. Then necessarily the first series exceeds the second series by four ranks. Then let us match this last end from the side of effects with the last end of these two series. The meaning of this matching is that the first of this series is aligned with the first of that series, the second of this with the second of that series. Then either every individual of the excessive series is matched with an individual of the deficient series to infinity, in which case the whole equals the part, and that is impossible. Or the deficient series terminates at the last end, so the deficient series is finite from that end, and the excessive series exceeds it by four, so the excessive series is also finite from that end. Thus causes and effects in the ascending ladder have an end and a beginning, and that prevents saying it has no end. Then that end, if it is possible in itself, is in need of another effecter, so it would not be an end—this is a contradiction. And if it is necessary in itself, then that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof against infinite regress (tasalsul). It uses a thought experiment. Assume an infinite chain of effects from a final effect backwards. Take two series from the final effect: one complete series, and another starting from the fifth rank. The first series is longer by four terms. Now align the two series from the 'last' end (the final effect). There are two possibilities: either the shorter series has enough terms to match all terms of the longer series to infinity, which would mean the whole equals the part—a contradiction. Or the shorter series runs out, meaning it is finite from that end, and then the longer series, being exactly four more, would also be finite. Thus the chain must have a first term. That first term, if possible, requires another cause and thus is not truly first; if necessary, it is the necessary existent.

Key Terms
البرهان الثالث
تسلسل
أسباب
مسببات
الأثر الأخير
طرف
مبدأ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof assumes that an actual infinite exists and tries to disprove it.
Correction: The proof is a reductio argument: it assumes infinite regress for the sake of argument and shows it leads to contradiction. It does not presuppose the existence of an actual infinite.

Checkpoint
In the third proof, what does the argument conclude about the series if the shorter series terminates?
A) Both series must be infinite
B) The longer series also becomes finite
C) The longer series remains infinite
D) The series has no beginning

Key Takeaway
Infinite regress of causes leads to logical contradiction, proving the necessity of a first cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the chain of events that led to your existence right now, do you truly believe it must end in a self-sufficient Being, or do you sometimes act as if the world explains itself? What would change if you lived every moment aware that your life depends on a Necessary Existent?
?
Razi's proof shows that an infinite regress is absurd. But in your daily life, do you ever delay accountability by assuming things will resolve themselves indefinitely? How would facing the reality of a finite chain of causes move you to take spiritual responsibility today?
?
If the first cause must be necessary, then your existence is not accidental but a direct gift from that Necessary Being. Are you willing to let that truth reframe how you spend the next hour? Or will you revert to treating your life as if it were self-caused?

Restating the Core Proof: Possibility Leads to the Necessary
Razi summarises the foundational proof via possibility and refutation of regress

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the classic kalam argument from possibility to the necessary existent.

Core Idea
Every possible being demands an external cause, halting only at the Necessary Being.

Imam Razi now restates the central argument for God's existence in a tight, logical form. Having established the impossibility of circularity and infinite regress in earlier premises, he applies them here to the division of existents into necessary and possible. This proof is the bedrock of Ash'ari theology, and Razi presents it with precision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic aloud or silently, then read the literal translation. Let the argument's flow sink in before breaking it down.
Notice how dense and structured each sentence is.
2
Identify the two categories of existents
Razi divides all existents into two: necessary in itself and possible in itself. Understand what each means.
The necessary cannot not exist; the possible can either exist or not.
3
Trace the logical alternatives
If there is a necessary existent, the proof is done. If all are possible, each needs a cause. But chains cannot go back indefinitely or in a circle.
Focus on the phrase 'al-dawr wa al-tasalsul batilan'.
4
Conclude the argument
The only way out of regress is a necessary existent that ends the chain. This is the sought conclusion.
That is the 'mublagh' (required).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ظهرت هذه المقدمات فلنرجع إلى أصل البرهان المذكور على وجود إثبات واجب الوجود، وهو أنه لا شك في وجود الموجودات وكل موجود فهو إما واجب لذاته وإما ممكن لذاته فإن حصل في الموجودات موجود واجب لذاته فذلك المطلوب، وإن كان ذلك الموجود ممكنًا افتقر إلى مؤثر . والدور والتسلسل باطلان، فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته، وذلك هو المطلوب.
And when these premises are evident, let us return to the original proof mentioned concerning the existence of the necessary of existence, which is that there is no doubt about the existence of existents. Every existent is either necessary in itself or possible in itself. If among existents there is an existent that is necessary in itself, then that is what is sought. And if that existent is possible, it is in need of an effecter. Circularity and infinite regress are both invalid. So it is necessary to terminate at an existent that is necessary of existence in itself, and that is what was sought.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage provides a concise summary of the classic kalam argument for God's existence. It begins by stating the obvious fact that things exist. Then it divides all existents into two categories: necessary in itself (wajib bi-dhatihi) and possible in itself (mumkin bi-dhatihi). If there is a necessary existent, the argument is done. If all existents are possible, then each possible requires an effecter (mu'aththir). But if we posit a chain of possible beings, we must avoid both circularity (dawr) and infinite regress (tasalsul), the impossibility of which was established in earlier proofs. Therefore the chain must terminate in a necessary existent. This is the foundational proof from possibility (imkan) used widely in Ash'ari kalam.

Key Terms

واجب لذاته
مؤثر
دور
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof assumes the universe is created and had a beginning in time.
Correction: The proof only uses logical categories (necessary vs. possible) and causal dependency, not temporal creation. It works even for a beginningless universe because possibility requires a cause regardless of time.

Checkpoint
What are the two invalid scenarios that must be avoided in the chain of possible causes?
A) Tawatur and Ishtirak
B) Qiyas and Ijma
C) Dawr (circularity) and Tasalsul (infinite regress)
D) Ta'til and Tashbih

Key Takeaway
The core kalam proof for God's existence avoids circularity and infinite regress by concluding that only a necessary existent can terminate the chain of possible beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This proof shows that the chain of possible causes must end with a Necessary Being. In your daily life, where do you find yourself relying on 'possible' things—people, possessions, or status—as if they were ultimate? How can you shift your ultimate reliance to the Necessary Being Allah?
?
The proof eliminates circularity and infinite regression. Reflect: Are there areas in your life where you rationalize in circles, avoiding the straight path to accountability before Allah? What would it look like to break that cycle today?
?
Imam Razi says 'there is no doubt about the existence of existents.' Do you live with that same certainty about the existence of Allah as the foundation of your reality? Or have you allowed doubt to creep in, affecting your actions and priorities? Commit to one concrete step that aligns your life with the certainty that only Allah is necessary.

The Second Method: Contingency of Attributes and the Similarity of Bodies
Imam Razi introduces the second proof, based on contingency of attributes, and states the first two premises.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second method for proving the Necessary Existent, which argues that the contingency of attributes in bodies requires an external specifier, and why the first two premises are crucial.

Core Idea
Bodies are equal in essence; their differing attributes demand an external cause.

Imam Razi now presents a second proof for the Necessary Existent, distinct from the earlier argument based on atoms. This method focuses on the attributes of bodies: since all bodies share the same essence, any attribute could belong to any body. The fact that each body has specific attributes therefore requires an external specifier. Razi outlines the first two premises of this proof, though the passage breaks off before the final premise and conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Note how Razi structures the proof: an introduction, the core argument, then the premises.
The passage ends mid-sentence after the second premise. This is a page break.
2
Identify the two premises
Razi states the proof rests on three premises. Find the first premise (bodies are equal in complete quiddity) and the second premise (since bodies are similar, what is possible for one is possible for the other). Understand how each is argued.
The first premise is supported by a division argument. The second premise is simply stated.
3
Reflect on the incomplete proof
Consider that the third premise and conclusion are missing. Based on the pattern of kalam proofs, what might the third premise be? Hint: it usually links the need for a specifier to the existence of the Necessary.
This lesson covers only the first two premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثاني في إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته : الاستدلال عليه بإمكان الصفات وتقريره أن نقول : الأجسام متساوية في الماهية والحقيقة، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك كان كل صفة اتصف بها الجسم أمكن اتصاف سائر الأجسام بها، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك افتقر كل واحد من الأجسام في اتصافه بصفته المخصوصة إلى مخصص ومرجح. وهذا البرهان مبني على ثلاث مقدمات: المقدمة الأولى : الأجسام متساوية في تمام الماهية : وأجود ما يمكن أن يذكر في هذا الباب أن الجسم يمكن تقسيمه إلى الفلكي وإلى العنصري، وإلى الكثيف وإلى اللطيف، ومورد القسمة يجب أن يكون مشتركًا فيه بين الأقسام، فإذن كونه جسما قدر مشترك فيه بين اللطيف والكثيف، والفلك والعنصر والحار والبارد، وهذا الذي وقع الامتياز فيه هو صفات الجسم، فثبت أن الأجسام متساوية في ذواتها وحقائقها والاختلاف الحاصل بينها ليس إلا في صفاتها وأعراضها، وفي هذا المقام أبحاث غامضة عميقة لا تليق بهذا المختصر. المقدمة الثانية : وهي أن الأجسام لما كانت متماثلة في ذواتها وحقائقها وجب أن يصح على كل واحد منها ما صح على الأخر :
The Second Method in Proving the Necessary of Existence in Itself: Inference upon it through the contingency of attributes. Its formulation: We say: Bodies are equal in quiddity and reality. When that is the case, every attribute with which a body is qualified, it is possible for other bodies to be qualified with it. When that is the case, each body, in its being qualified with its particular attribute, is in need of a specifier and rajih (preponderator). This proof is built upon three premises: The First Premise: Bodies are equal in complete quiddity. The best that can be mentioned in this regard is that the body can be divided into celestial and elemental, dense and subtle. The subject of the division must be common among the divisions. Therefore its being a body is a common factor among the subtle and the dense, the celestial and the element, the hot and the cold. What is the point of differentiation among them is the attributes of the body. Thus it is established that bodies are equal in their essences and realities, and the difference occurring between them is only in their attributes and accidents. In this station, there are profound and deep investigations not befitting this abridgment. The Second Premise: That since bodies are similar in their essences and realities, it must be valid for each one of them what is valid for the other:
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage introduces the second method for proving the necessary existent, based on the contingency of accidents (imkan al-sifat). It argues that all bodies share the same essence (mahiyya) of 'body-hood'. Since they are equal in essence, any attribute found in one body could, in principle, be in another. Therefore, when a body has a particular attribute (e.g., being hot or cold), it requires a specifier (mukhasiss) or preponderator (murajih) to bring about that attribute rather than another. This specifier must be external. The proof is said to rest on three premises. The first premise is argued: bodies are equal in their complete quiddity because 'body' is a common term across all types of bodies (celestial/elemental, dense/subtle). The differences are only in attributes and accidents. The second premise is then stated: since bodies are alike, whatever is possible for one is possible for the other. The text ends before the third premise and conclusion are given.

الماهية
مخصص
مرجح
قسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the contingency of attributes argument relies on the impossibility of atoms or accidents in bodies.
Correction: Razi's argument does not depend on atomism. It uses the equality of bodies in essence and the contingency of their attributes to infer the need for an external specifier.

Checkpoint

A) Because the body is necessary in itself
B) Because the body's attributes are eternal
C) Because bodies are equal in essence, so any attribute could be present; the presence of a specific attribute requires a cause
D) Because bodies are made of atoms

Key Takeaway
The second method proves the Necessary Existent by showing that the contingency of attributes in bodies necessitates an external specifier.

?
The proof demands an external specifier for every attribute. How does that challenge your daily assumption that you control your own outcomes? Will you today, in every small decision, consciously acknowledge that your very existence and attributes are contingent on Allah?

The Contingency Argument for a Necessary Being
Razi restates the classic contingency argument for a Necessary Being.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the contingency argument (burhan al-imkan) and how it demonstrates the necessity of a Necessary Being.

Core Idea
The contingency argument shows that the existence of contingent beings leads inevitably to a Necessary Being.

In this passage, Imam al-Razi distills the classic contingency argument into a few clear steps. He begins with the undeniable fact that things exist, then divides all existents into two categories: those that are necessary in themselves and those that are possible. By eliminating circularity and infinite regress, he concludes that a Necessary Being must exist. This argument is foundational in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the literal English translation. Let the flow of the argument sink in.
Notice how Razi uses concise phrasing to present a complete logical argument.
2
Identify the two categories and logical steps
Find the division of existents into necessary and possible. Then trace the two cases: if a necessary being exists, the goal is reached; if a possible being exists, it requires a cause. Follow the reasoning that rejects circularity and infinite regress.
This step helps you see the argument's structure: premise → division → cases → elimination of bad options → conclusion.
3
Reflect on why circularity and infinite regress are false
Consider why circularity (A causes B, B causes A) and infinite regress (an endless chain of causes) are logically impossible for explaining existence. This leads to the necessity of a self-sufficient cause.
The argument's power lies in ruling out alternatives, leaving only the Necessary Being.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا ظهرت هذه المقدمات فلنرجع إلى أصل البرهان المذكور على وجود إثبات واجب الوجود، وهو أنه لا شك في وجود الموجودات وكل موجود فهو إما واجب لذاته وإما ممكن لذاته فإن حصل في الموجودات موجود واجب لذاته فذلك المطلوب، وإن كان ذلك الموجود ممكنًا افتقر إلى مؤثر . والدور والتسلسل باطلان، فلا بد من الانتهاء إلى موجود واجب الوجود لذاته، وذلك هو المطلوب.
And when these premises become clear, let us return to the origin of the mentioned proof for establishing the existence of the Necessary Being. It is that there is no doubt about the existence of existents, and every existent is either necessary in itself or possible in itself. If among existents there exists an existent necessary in itself, that is the sought. If that existent is possible, it needs an effect. Circularity and infinite regress are false, so one must end at an existent necessary in itself, and that is the sought.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a concise restatement of the classic contingency argument (burhan al-imkan). Razi first asserts the obviousness of existence. He divides all existents into two categories: necessary in itself (wajib li-dhatihi) and possible in itself (mumkin li-dhatihi). If a necessary being exists, the proof is satisfied. If a possible being exists, it requires a cause. Since circularity and infinite regress are invalid, the chain of causes must terminate in a being that is necessary in itself. This is the desired conclusion.

Key Terms
واجب لذاته (necessary in itself)
ممكن لذاته (possible in itself)
دور (circularity)
تسلسل (infinite regress)
مؤثر (efficient cause)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that every contingent being has a cause, which is doubted by some.
Correction: The argument is not about individual causation but about the dependency of the entire set of contingent beings on a necessary being. It uses the impossibility of an infinite regress of causes to force a necessary endpoint.

Checkpoint

A) A possible being
B) A necessary being
C) An infinite series
D) A first possible cause

Key Takeaway
The contingency argument demonstrates that the existence of contingent beings logically requires a Necessary Being, because an infinite regress or circularity of causes is impossible.

?

?

Second Proof: Argument from Possibility of Attributes and First Premise
Razi introduces the second proof and establishes the first premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi establishes the first premise of the second proof for the existence of the Necessary Being, namely that all bodies are identical in essence.

Core Idea
Bodies share one essence; their diverse attributes demand a particularizer.

Razi presents a new proof based on the possibility of attributes. He argues that since all bodies are identical in their quiddity, any attribute could belong to any body; therefore, the specific attribute of each body requires a cause that particularizes it. This lesson examines the first premise: bodies are equal in essence and reality.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: Razi first states the proof, then explains its foundation, and finally gives the first premise.
The proof is based on three premises, but this lesson only covers the first.
2
Identify the first premise
Find the sentence where Razi explicitly states 'bodies are equal in their complete quiddity'. Understand that this is the foundation of the argument.
3
Examine the justification
See how Razi supports the premise: body is divided into types (celestial/elemental, dense/subtle). The division implies a common genus 'body'. Differences are only in accidental attributes.
This reasoning shows that the essence of body is uniform across all bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثاني في إثبات واجب الوجود لذاته : الاستدلال عليه بإمكان الصفات هو وتقريره أن نقول : الأجسام متساوية في الماهية والحقيقة، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك كان كل صفة اتصف بها الجسم أمكن اتصاف سائر الأجسام بها، ومتى كان الأمر كذلك افتقر كل واحد من الأجسام في اتصافه بصفته المخصوصة إلى مخصص ومرجح. وهذا البرهان مبني على ثلاث مقدمات: المقدمة الأولى : الأجسام متساوية في تمام الماهية : وأجود ما يمكن أن يذكر في هذا الباب أن الجسم يمكن تقسيمه إلى الفلكي وإلى العنصري، وإلى الكثيف وإلى اللطيف، ومورد القسمة يجب أن يكون مشتركًا فيه بين الأقسام، فإذن كونه جسما قدر مشترك فيه بين اللطيف والكثيف، والفلك والعنصر والحار والبارد، وهذا الذي وقع الامتياز فيه هو صفات الجسم، فثبت أن الأجسام متساوية في ذواتها وحقائقها والاختلاف الحاصل بينها ليس إلا في صفاتها وأعراضها، وفي هذا المقام أبحاث غامضة عميقة لا تليق بهذا المختصر.
The second way of proving the Necessary Being in itself: the inference from the possibility of attributes. Its formulation is that we say: Bodies are equal in quiddity and reality. When this is the case, every attribute with which a body is qualified is possible to be qualified by other bodies. When this is the case, each body, in being qualified by its specific attribute, needs a particularizer and preponderator. This proof is based on three premises. The first premise: Bodies are equal in their complete quiddity. The best that can be mentioned in this regard is that body can be divided into celestial and elemental, and into dense and subtle. The subject of division must be common among the divisions. Thus, being a body is a common factor between the subtle and the dense, the celestial and the element, the hot and the cold. That which differentiates them is the attributes of the body. So it is established that bodies are equal in their essences and realities, and the difference among them is only in their attributes and accidents. In this topic there are deep and subtle investigations not suitable for this abridgment.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is the introduction of the second proof (al-tariq al-thani) for the existence of a necessary being, based on the possibility of attributes (imkan al-sifat). Razi first states that all bodies are equal in quiddity (mahiyya) and reality (haqiqa). If so, any attribute found in one body could in principle be found in others. Therefore, the specific attribute in each body requires a particularizer (mukhassis) and preponderator (murajjih) that selects that attribute. This proof rests on three premises. The first premise is that bodies are identical in essence. To support this, Razi notes that body is divided into types (celestial/elemental, dense/subtle), and the division requires a common genus. The differences are only in accidents (a'rad). Thus, essences are uniform. Razi notes that deeper investigations exist but are omitted here.

إمكان الصفات
مخصص
ماهية
جسم
قسمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The premise claims that bodies are identical in every respect, including their physical properties.
Correction: Razi explicitly states that bodies are equal only in their essential nature (dhat and haqiqa); differences are in attributes and accidents (a'rad).

If all bodies are equal in essence, then no attribute is more 'natural' than another. This means your very existence at every moment needs the Particularizer. Are you living as if you are self-sufficient? Commit right now to recite 'La hawla wa la quwwata illa billah' with understanding as a recognition of this need.

The Second Premise: Uniform Essences and the Objection from Individuality
Razi establishes the second premise, answers an objection about individuality, and deduces far-reaching implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that all bodies share the same possibilities, and how he refutes the objection that individual identity restricts those possibilities.

Core Idea
Essences are uniform; individuality does not limit essential possibility.

Razi builds his proof for the possibility of attributes on a foundational premise: if bodies are alike in essence, then what is possible for one is possible for all. However, a natural objection arises: individuals within a species are distinct, so why can't individuality itself restrict possibilities? Razi meets this objection head-on, using subtle distinctions between necessary and accidental attributes. This lesson unpacks that crucial argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the premise and its justification
Start by reading Razi's statement of the second premise and why he argues that uniform essences imply uniform possibilities. Notice the two cases: receptivity as necessary concomitant or as accidental.
2
Examine the objection about individuality
Read the objector's challenge: individuals differ in their 'determination' (ta'ayyun), and this might condition or prevent certain attributes. Understand why this seems to break the premise.
The key term here is تعين – determination/individuality.
3
Follow Razi's two-part answer
Razi answers by first noting that if determination is a non-entity, the objection dissolves. Then, if it is a positive entity, it is accidental, not necessary. Therefore, any attribute conditioned by it is also accidental and does not undermine the essential possibility.
This is the heart of the answer.
4
Consider the radical implications
Razi draws conclusions: all bodies, including celestial spheres, can undergo change, and even the structure of the universe can transform. This premise is crucial for both theology and eschatology.
5
Reflect on the overall importance
Consider why this premise is 'of great benefit in knowing the beginning and the resurrection.' How does it support belief in God as creator and in the afterlife as described in the Quran?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثانية : وهي أن الأجسام لما كانت متماثلة في ذواتها وحقائقها وجب أن يصح على كل واحد منها ما صح على الأخر : وذلك لأن قابلية هذا العرض إن كانت من لوازم تلك الماهية فإذا حصلت تلك الماهية لزم حصول تلك القابلية، فوجب أن يصح على كل واحد منها ما صح على واحد منها، وإن لم يكن من لوازمها كان من عوارضها، فيعود الكلام في قبلية تلك القابلية؛ فإن كانت أيضًا من العوارض يلزم إما التسلسل وإما الانتهاء إلى قابلية لازمة لتلك الماهية وحينئذ يحصل المطلوب. فإن قال قائل : أفراد النوع الواحد وإن كانت متساوية في ماهية ذلك النوع لكنها غير متساوية في كون كل واحد منها غير ذلك الشخص، وذلك المعين، وذلك لأن تعين هذا إن كان حاصلا لذلك فهذا هو عين ذاك، فلم يكن مغايرا له قثبت أ. تعين كل واحد من الأفراد غير حاصل للفرد الآخر. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول : لم لا يجوز أن يكون ذلك التعين شرطا لتلك القابلية في هذا الفرد أو يكون تعين الفرد الآخر مانعا من هذه القابلية، وإذا كان الأمر كذلك لم يلزم أن يصح على كل واحد من أفراد النوع الواحد ما صح على فرد من أفراده. والجواب : أما على قول من يقول : التعين أمر عدمي فالسؤال ساقط، وأما عند من يقول إنه أمر ثبوتي فجوابه : أن ذلك التعين ليس من لوازم تلك الماهية، وإلا لزم أن يكون ذلك النوع في ذلك الشخص، لكنه ليس الأمر كذلك ؛ لأن هذا الكلام مفروض في نوع حصل له أفراد كثير بالفعل، فإذن ذلك التعين الذي به صار ذلك الشخص ذلك المعين أمر غير لازم لتلك الماهية ، فهو عارض جائز الزوال، وكل ما كان عارضا لذلك الشخص بسبب ذلك التعين وجب أيضًا أن يكون جائز الزوال، وذلك يقتضي أن يكون اتصاف كل واحد من الأجسام بما اتصف به أمرًا ممكنا جائزا . وعند هذا ظهر أن كل ما على العناصر فمثله على الأفلاك، فكان يصح الخرق والالتئام والفطور والانشقاق جائزا على الأفلاك، وكما يمكن انقلاب الأرض ماء والماء هواء والهواء نارًا فكذلك يمكن انقلاب الفلك أرضًا والأرض فلكاً، وكما يمكن كون الفلك محيطا بالأرض والأرض مركزاً، فكذلك يمكن انقلاب كون الأرض محيطا والفلك مركزاً، وهذه المقدمة كما أنها عظيمة المنفعة في معرفة المبدأ فهي أيضًا عظيمة المنفعة في معرفة المعاد، وتصحيح ما ورد في القرآن من أحوال القيامة.
The second premise: Since bodies are similar in their essences and realities, it must be that what is valid for one of them is valid for the other. This is because the receptivity to this accident: if it is among the necessary concomitants of that quiddity, then when that quiddity occurs, the receptivity necessarily occurs, so it must be valid for each one what is valid for one. If it is not among its necessary concomitants but among its accidents, then the discussion returns to the receptivity of that receptivity. If that too is among the accidents, then either infinite regress or ending at a receptivity that is necessary for that quiddity follows, and then the desired is obtained. If someone says: Individuals of a single species are equal in the quiddity of that species, but they are not equal in that each one is other than that person and that determinate individual, because the determination (ta'ayyun) of this one: if it were to that, then this would be identical to that, so it would not be different from it. So it is established that the determination of each individual is not obtained for the other individual. If this is established, we say: Why is it not permissible that that determination is a condition for that receptivity in this individual, or that the determination of the other individual is a prevention of this receptivity? If this is the case, then it does not necessarily follow that what is valid for one individual of the species is valid for all individuals. The answer: As for the view that determination is a non-entity (amr 'adami), the objection falls. As for the view that it is a positive entity (amr thubuti), the answer is that this determination is not among the necessary concomitants of that quiddity, otherwise the species would be in that person, but it is not so; because this discussion is assumed for a species that actually has many individuals. So that determination by which that person becomes that determinate individual is not necessary for that quiddity; it is an accidental (arid) entity subject to cessation. And everything that is accidental to that person due to that determination must also be subject to cessation. This entails that the qualification of each body with what it is qualified is a possible contingent matter. At this point, it becomes clear that whatever is valid for the elements is likewise valid for the celestial spheres. Thus, it is valid that rupture, recombination, splitting, and cleavage are possible for the celestial spheres. Just as it is possible for earth to become water, water to become air, air to become fire, so also it is possible for the celestial sphere to become earth and earth to become the sphere. Just as it is possible for the sphere to encompass the earth and the earth to be the center, so also it is possible for the earth to encompass and the sphere to be the center. This premise, as it is of great benefit in knowing the beginning (al-mabda'), it is also of great benefit in knowing the resurrection (al-ma'ad) and in affirming what is mentioned in the Qur'an about the conditions of the Last Day.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents the second premise of the proof from possibility of attributes. Razi argues that if bodies are similar in essence, then any attribute possible for one is possible for another. He explains that the receptivity to an attribute either is a necessary concomitant (lazim) of the essence (so it applies universally) or is accidental. If accidental, we need an explanation for the receptivity itself, leading to infinite regress or a necessary receptivity, which again implies universality. An objector then raises the issue of individual identity (ta'ayyun): individuals of a species are distinct, and this distinctness might condition or prevent certain attributes. Razi gives two answers: if determination is non-entity (as some hold), the objection is moot. If it is a positive entity, it is accidental to the essence, not necessary, so any attribute conditioned by it is also accidental and does not block the essential possibility. Therefore, all bodies share the same possibilities. This leads to radical conclusions: celestial bodies can undergo changes like elemental bodies, and the universe could be transformed. This premise is useful both for theology (knowledge of God as creator) and eschatology (knowledge of resurrection and Quranic descriptions).

Key Terms
قابلية (receptivity)
عرض (accident)
لازم (necessary concomitant)
عارض (accidental)
تعين (determination/individuality)
عدمي (non-entity)
ثبوتي (positive entity)
أفلاك (celestial spheres)
عناصر (elements)
خرق والالتئام (rupture and recombination)
معاد (resurrection)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that Razi is saying all bodies are identical in every respect, so they must have the same actual attributes.
Correction: Razi only claims similarity in essence and potential possibility, not actual identicality. Individuality distinguishes them in existence but does not affect essential possibilities.

Checkpoint
How does Razi answer the objection that individual determination (ta'ayyun) prevents universal possibility?
A) He denies individuality exists
B) He says determination is not a necessary concomitant of the essence
C) He agrees and modifies the premise
D) He invokes divine will

Key Takeaway
Razi's second premise establishes that all bodies share the same essential possibilities, with individuality being an accidental factor that does not restrict those possibilities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If every body shares the same essential possibilities, including the celestial spheres, what does that mean for the parts of your life you consider 'fixed' or 'unchangeable'? What would change if you truly believed that your current limitations are accidental, not essential?
?
Razi argues that individuality does not restrict what is possible for you. What have you told yourself is 'just how I am' that might actually be an accidental condition you have the power to change?
?
The second premise leads to the conclusion that even the highest heavens can be transformed. Are you living as if you are part of that same reality, or do you act as if some things in your character or situation are beyond the reach of divine transformation? What would you ask Allah to change in you today if you believed nothing in creation is essentially fixed?

Third Premise: Every Body Requires a Specific Location
Razi states the third premise: bodies require a specific location.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the third premise: that every existing body must occupy a specific spatial location, and why this premise is foundational to the cosmological argument for God's existence.

Core Idea
Every body must be somewhere specific—space is essential to physical existence.

Imam Razi begins his third premise with a simple but profound claim: every body that exists must have a specific spatial location. This idea may seem obvious, but it carries deep implications for understanding the contingency of the physical world. Although the text breaks off, the premise sets the stage for an argument that will lead to the necessity of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic sentence once. Notice how Razi states the premise clearly: 'every body that exists must have a specific space.'
The text repeats 'specific space' and then cuts off—this is a textual break.
2
Understand the key terms
The term 'حيز' (hayyiz) means space or location, and 'معين' (mu'ayyan) means specific or particular. Together they stress that a body's location is not arbitrary but determinate.
In Islamic kalam, 'hayyiz' is a technical term for the three-dimensional region a body occupies.
3
Reflect on the premise's role
Ask yourself: if every body occupies a specific location, what does that imply about bodies? Why might this be important for proving God's existence? The premise highlights that bodies are limited and conditioned by space.
This premise often leads to the idea that bodies require a cause for their specific location, setting up the contingency argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الثالثة : وهي أن كل جسم يوجد فلا بد له من حيز معين، من حيز معين ولا بد له من
The third premise: that every body that exists must have a specific space, a specific space and it must have...
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage begins the third premise of the second proof. Razi asserts that every existing body necessarily occupies a specific spatial location (hayyiz mu'ayyan). The text breaks off, but the premise likely continues to argue that this specific location requires a cause (since bodies are similar in essence but differ in location). The incomplete nature makes it difficult to fully explain; it requires consultation of the complete work.

Proof Through Contingency of Originated Things
Razi argues that origination implies contingency, and contingency demands an external influencer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi proves the existence of the Creator by showing that originated bodies are contingent and therefore require an external influencer.

Core Idea
Originated things are possible, not necessary; possibility signals dependence on a Creator.

Razi now presents a third argument for the Creator, rooted in the nature of originated things. He builds on the established fact that bodies are originated, then argues that every originated thing must have an originator because it is contingent. This passage focuses on the crucial step: proving that origination implies contingency, which in turn demands an external cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the logical structure: bodies are originated → originated is contingent → contingent needs an influencer.
2
Identify the logical chain
Break down the argument: (1) bodies are originated, (2) every originated has an originator, (3) the proof that originated is contingent because its essence admits non-existence and existence, (4) the contingent needs an external influencer.
The reference to the 'first proof' ties this argument to the earlier demonstration that possible things need an external cause.
3
Reflect on contingency and necessity
Consider why origination proves contingency: before existence, the thing was non-existent, so its essence could accept non-existence; after existence, it exists, so its essence could accept existence. This dual reception is the definition of the possible (mumkin).
If something were necessary, its essence would not admit non-existence at any time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثالث في إثبات العلم بالصانع تعالى الاستدلال عليه بحدوث الجواهر والأجسام وتقريره ثبت أن الأجسام محدثة، وكل محدث فله محدث، فالأجسام مفتقرة إلى المحدث، أما بيان أن الأجسام محدثة فقد تقدم، وأما بيان أن كل محدث فله محدث فللناس ههنا طريقان الأول : أن كل محدث فهو جائز الوجود لذاته، وكل ما كان جائز الوجود لذاته فهو مفتقر إلى المؤثر، وإنما قلنا : إن كل ما كان محدثًا فهو جائز الوجود لذاته وذلك لأن كل ما كان محدثًا فقد كان قبل وجوده معدوماً، ولو لم تكن ماهيته قابلة للعدم لما كان معدوماً. وأيضًا كل محدث فإنه بعد حدوثه موصوف بالوجود، ولو لم تكن ماهيته قابلة للوجود لما كان كذلك، فثبت أن كل ما كان محدثًا فإن ماهيته قابلة للعدم وقابلة للوجود، ولا معنى للممكن إلا ذلك، فثبت أن كل محدث فإنه ممكن الوجود والعدم لذاته، أما أن كل ممكن فإنه مفتقر إلى المؤثر فلما مر تقريره في البرهان الأول، وبالله التوفيق.
The third way in proving knowledge of the Creator, exalted is He: inference to Him through the temporal origination (ḥudūth) of substances (jawāhir) and bodies. Its formulation: It is established that bodies are originated (muḥdatha), and every originated has an originator (muḥdith). So bodies are in need of the originator. As for the demonstration that bodies are originated, it has preceded. As for the demonstration that every originated has an originator, people here have two ways. The first: that every originated is contingent in itself (jāʾiz al-wujūd li-dhātihi), and everything that is contingent in itself is in need of an influencer (muʾaththir). And we say that every originated is contingent in itself because every originated was non-existent before its existence, and if its essence (māhīya) were not receptive to non-existence, it would not have been non-existent. Also, every originated, after its origination, is described by existence, and if its essence were not receptive to existence, it would not be so. So it is established that every originated has an essence receptive to non-existence and receptive to existence, and there is no meaning to the possible (mumkin) except that. So it is established that every originated is possible in existence and non-existence in itself. As for that every possible is in need of an influencer, its formulation has passed in the first proof. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents the third way (ṭarīq thālith) to prove the existence of the Creator (al-Ṣāniʿ), using the origination of substances and bodies. The proof is structured: first, it has already been established that bodies are originated (muḥdatha). Then it needs to show that every originated thing has an originator. Razi mentions two ways (though only the first is given here). The first way: every originated thing is contingent (jāʾiz al-wujūd) in itself. The argument for contingency is: before existence, it was non-existent, so its essence must be capable of non-existence; after existence, it is described by existence, so its essence must be capable of existence. Therefore, its essence admits both non-existence and existence, which is the definition of the possible (mumkin). Then, every possible needs an external influencer (muʾaththir) to bring it into existence, as established in the first proof. Thus, bodies require an originator, which is the Creator.

Key Terms
حدوث
محدث
جائز الوجود
ممكن
مؤثر
ماهية
صانع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that originated things could become necessary of themselves; Razi argues that before existence they were non-existent, so they cannot be necessary.
Correction: The passage shows that origination implies contingency because the essence admitted non-existence before. If it were necessary, it could not have been non-existent.

Checkpoint

A) Because it was created by God.
B) Because it was non-existent before and then existed, showing its essence admits both states.
C) Because it has a specific shape and place.
D) Because it is composed of atoms.

Key Takeaway
The possibility (contingency) of originated things points directly to the need for a Creator who brings them into existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If you truly internalized that every single thing you depend on—your health, your wealth, your relationships—is contingent and could cease to exist at any moment, would you still cling to them as if they were permanent? What would it look like to anchor your security only in the Necessary Being?
?
Before you existed, you were nothing, and your essence was receptive to non-existence. Does that thought humble you? How does it change the way you view your own accomplishments and status when you know they are gifts from the Creator, not self-generated?
?
Razi argues that everything originated bears the mark of need. Look at your own life: where do you most resist admitting your need for the Creator? In what areas do you act as if you are self-sufficient? What would change if you confessed your contingency in those areas today?

All Bodies Need a Determiner: The Argument from Contingency
Razi argues for a non-bodily necessary being from the determination of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the specific properties of bodies are contingent, requiring a non-bodily determiner to avoid infinite regress, leading to a necessary being.

Core Idea
Every body's properties are contingent; an infinite chain of determiners is impossible, so there must be a non-bodily necessary being.

Razi presents a concise argument for the existence of a necessary being based on the determination of bodies. He begins by noting that every body's specific properties are contingent—they could have been otherwise. Contingent things require a cause to specify them. If that cause is itself a body, it too would need a cause, leading to an infinite regress, which is impossible. Therefore, the chain must terminate in a non-bodily necessary being.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice how Razi builds his argument step by step.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the key concepts
Find the terms 'al-ja'iz' (contingent), 'al-murajjih' (preponderator), 'al-mukhassis' (determiner), and 'al-tasalsul' (infinite regress). Understand their roles.
These are technical terms in kalam.
3
Trace the logical flow
Follow the argument: Premise: body properties are contingent → Contingent needs a determiner → If determiner is body, infinite regress → Infinite regress impossible → Therefore non-bodily determiner.
You can use the guided breakdown to help.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider why Razi rejects infinite regress and how this argument leads to a non-bodily necessary being.
This argument is foundational for proving God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد دللنا على أن اختصاص كل واحد من الأجسام بصفته المعينة لا بد وأن يكون من الجائزات ولا بد للجائز من مرجح ومخصص فثبت بهذا افتقار جميع الأجسام في أحيازها وصفاتها إلى مخصص ومرجح، ثم ذلك المرجح إن كان جسما أيضا في حيزه المعين وشكله المعين وصفته المعينة إلى مخصص ومرجح، ثم ذلك المرجح إلى مخصص ومرجح، وذلك يفضي إلى التسلسل وهو محال كما سبق، فثبت افتقار جميع الأجسام في صفاتها إلى موجود ليس بجسم ولا جسماني، وإذا أردنا أن نبين أن ذلك الموجود واجب الوجود لذاته رجعنا إلى بعض ما ذكرناه في البرهان الأول.
And we have demonstrated that the specification of each body with its particular property must be among contingent matters, and the contingent necessarily requires a preponderator (murajjih) and a determiner (mukhassis). Thus it is established thereby that all bodies, in their positions and properties, are dependent upon a determiner and preponderator. Then if that preponderator is also a body, then it too, in its particular position, shape, and property, is dependent upon a determiner and preponderator, and that leads to an infinite regress, which is impossible as previously shown. So it is established that all bodies, in their properties, are dependent upon an existent that is not a body nor a corporeal (jismani). And if we wish to demonstrate that that existent is necessary in itself (wajib al-wujud li-dhatih), we refer to some of what we mentioned in the first proof.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for the existence of a non-bodily necessary being from the determination of bodies. It begins by stating that every body's specific property (such as its shape or location) is contingent, meaning it could have been otherwise. Contingent things require a preponderator or determiner to specify them. If that determiner is itself a body, then it too would require a determiner, leading to an infinite regress (tasalsul), which is impossible. Therefore, the chain must end with an existent that is neither a body nor a corporeal being. This being is then identified as the necessary existent, though the proof for its necessity is referenced from a previous argument.

Key Terms
الجائز (al-ja'iz)
المرجح (al-murajjih)
المخصص (al-mukhassis)
التسلسل (al-tasalsul)
واجب الوجود (wajib al-wujud)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument implies an infinite chain of causes is acceptable.
Correction: Razi explicitly states that infinite regress (tasalsul) is impossible (muhal), so it cannot be accepted.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, what is the consequence if the determiner of a body's properties is itself a body?
A) It leads to an infinite regress which is impossible
B) It is acceptable because bodies can be self-determining
C) It proves that bodies are eternal
D) It shows that the determiner must be a different type of body

Key Takeaway
Razi shows that an infinite regress of bodily determiners is impossible, so there must be a non-bodily necessary being that grounds all contingent bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every detail of your life—your health, wealth, even your next breath—is contingent and requires a determiner, why do you often live as if these things are self-caused or due to your own effort? How can you train your heart to see every good as coming directly from Allah's determination?
?
Razi argues that an infinite regress of causes is impossible. In your spiritual life, do you ever chase an infinite regress of excuses—'I’ll repent later,' 'I’ll become better when...'—without ever reaching the ultimate cause of change? What would it mean for you to stop the regress and turn to the Necessary Being right now?
?
The argument concludes that all bodies depend on a non-bodily necessary being. In what areas of your life do you still place ultimate trust in material things—career, relationships, possessions—rather than in the non-bodily Creator who alone is necessary? What concrete step can you take today to shift that trust?

The Third Proof: Temporality and Contingency
Razi presents the third proof for the Creator from temporality, detailing the first method that temporals are contingent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third rational proof for the existence of the Creator, which links the temporality of bodies to their contingency and thus to the need for an external effecter.

Core Idea
Temporality implies contingency, and contingency demands a Creator.

Imam Fakhr al-Din al-Razi now presents the third way to establish knowledge of the Creator. This argument builds on the earlier proof that bodies are temporal (haditha). The key move is to show that every temporal thing is contingent (ja'iz al-wujud) in itself, and therefore requires an external effecter (mu'aththir). This lesson unpacks that reasoning and connects it to the concept of possibility (imkan).

Guided Path
1
Understand the overall structure
Read the passage once to see how Razi structures the proof: he states the conclusion, then breaks down the demonstration into methods.
The passage references a prior proof for the temporality of bodies.
2
Follow the first method
Razi explains that every temporal is contingent in itself. Pay attention to his reasoning: because the temporal was non-existent before existence and came to exist, its essence must be receptive to both states.
This defines contingency as the essence being equally receptive to existence and non-existence.
3
Connect contingency to the need for an effecter
Razi asserts that everything contingent needs an effecter, and refers to the first proof for justification. Understand that this step completes the argument.
The first proof likely established that contingent beings require a cause.
4
Reflect on the argument's implications
Consider how this proof shows that the temporality of the physical world directly points to a Creator without relying on empirical observation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطريق الثالث في إثبات العلم بالصانع تعالى الاستدلال عليه بحدوث الجواهر والأجسام وتقريره ثبت أن الأجسام محدثة، وكل محدث فله محدث، فالأجسام مفتقرة إلى المحدث، أما بيان أن الأجسام محدثة فقد تقدم، وأما بيان أن كل محدث فله محدث فللناس ههنا طريقان الأول : أن كل محدث فهو جائز الوجود لذاته، وكل ما كان جائز الوجود لذاته فهو مفتقر إلى المؤثر، وإنما قلنا : إن كل ما كان محدثا فهو جائز الوجود لذاته وذلك لأن كل ما كان محدثا فقد كان قبل وجوده معدوما، ولو لم تكن ماهيته قابلة للعدم لما كان معدوما. وأيضا كل محدث فإنه بعد حدوثه موصوف بالوجود، ولو لم تكن ماهيته قابلة للوجود لما كان كذلك، فثبت أن كل ما كان محدثا فإن ماهيته قابلة للعدم وقابلة للوجود، ولا معنى للممكن إلا ذلك، فثبت أن كل محدث فإنه ممكن الوجود والعدم لذاته، أما أن كل ممكن فإنه مفتقر إلى المؤثر فلما مر تقريره في البرهان الأول، وبالله التوفيق.
The third way to prove knowledge of the Creator, may He be exalted, is to argue from the temporality (huduth) of substances (jawahir) and bodies (ajsam). Its formulation: it is established that bodies are temporal (muhdatha), and every temporal has a temporizer (muhdith), therefore bodies are in need of the temporizer. As for the demonstration that bodies are temporal, it has already preceded. As for the demonstration that every temporal has a temporizer, people have two methods here. The first: every temporal is contingent in itself (ja'iz al-wujud li-dhatih), and everything that is contingent in itself is in need of an effecter (mu'aththir). We say that every temporal is contingent in itself because every temporal was non-existent before its existence, and if its essence (mahiyya) were not receptive to non-existence, it would not have been non-existent. Also, every temporal, after its coming into existence, is described by existence, and if its essence were not receptive to existence, it would not be so. Thus it is established that every temporal has an essence that is receptive to both non-existence and existence, and the meaning of 'possible' (mumkin) is nothing other than that. So it is established that every temporal is possible in existence and non-existence in itself. As for the fact that every possible is in need of an effecter, its demonstration has already passed in the first proof. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents the third way (al-tariq al-thalith) for proving the existence of the Creator. The argument is based on the temporality of substances and bodies. It restates that bodies are temporal, and every temporal needs a temporizer. It then focuses on proving that every temporal needs a temporizer. The first method (al-tariq al-awwal) given here is that every temporal is contingent (ja'iz al-wujud) in itself, and every contingent needs an effecter. The reason a temporal is contingent is that it was non-existent before existence and then came to exist; this indicates that its essence is receptive to both existence and non-existence, which is the definition of possibility (imkan). Thus, all temporals are possible beings and therefore require an effecter to bring them into existence. The proof for the need of an effecter is referenced to a previous argument (al-burhan al-awwal).

Key Terms
الحدوث (al-huduth)
المحدث (al-muhdith)
الجائز الوجود (ja'iz al-wujud)
الممكن (al-mumkin)
الماهية (al-mahiyya)
المؤثر (al-mu'aththir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that 'temporal' and 'contingent' are the same thing, or that temporality alone proves the need for a Creator without further reasoning.
Correction: Razi shows that temporality implies contingency because the essence does not necessitate existence at all times. Only after showing that every temporal is contingent does he then invoke the principle that contingents need an effecter.

Checkpoint

B) Every temporal is contingent in itself
C) Every temporal is eternal
D) Every temporal is self-caused

Key Takeaway
The temporality of bodies proves their contingency, and contingency proves the need for a Creator.

The passage shows that your essence does not inherently possess existence; it is given to you. When you look at your own ambitions, achievements, and struggles, do you ever act as if existence and success are self-generated? What would it mean to acknowledge that even your next breath is a gift from the Effecter?
?
Razi argues that contingency demands a cause. Your own existence is a string of contingent moments. Are you living as if you are a necessary being, or are you humbly returning every moment to the One who sustains you? What one habit can you change today to reflect that dependency outwardly?

Objection: Why Cannot the Temporal Become Necessary in Itself?
Razi raises an objection with three points against the contingency argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three objections Razi raises against the contingency argument and how they challenge the need for an external temporizer.

Core Idea
The objection claims that temporal things might become necessary in themselves, challenging the need for a Creator.

Imam Razi presents a sophisticated objection to the standard argument that every temporal thing requires an external cause. He offers three distinct reasons why a thing could become necessary in itself at a particular moment, drawing analogies from accidents, instants of time, and the possibility of the world. This passage forces us to examine the foundations of the contingency argument more deeply.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the translation. Let the objector's own argument come through.
Notice the structure: the objector states a general possibility and then gives three proofs.
2
Analyze the first objection (accidents)
The objector uses the example of an accident: its non-existence in the second moment is necessary in itself. Ask: why does this imply that existence could also become necessary in itself?
This is an analogy from non-existence to existence.
3
Analyze the second objection (the 'now')
The objector points out that the present instant (al-an) is necessarily non-persistent. If this necessity is intrinsic, why can't other temporals have intrinsic necessity?
This extends the argument from accidents to time itself.
4
Analyze the third objection (the world's possibility)
The objector notes that the world's possibility in the future is not caused by an agent, since it exists regardless. Thus, temporal occurrence can be by itself.
This is the strongest point: it claims that something can become possible by itself.
5
Reflect on the overall challenge
Consider how these three points together challenge the idea that every temporal thing needs an external cause. Why might the contingency argument still be defensible?
This prepares you for the expected response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : لم لا يجوز أن يقال : إنه كان معدوما ثم انقلب في ذلك الوقت المعين واجبا لذاته؟ والذي يدل على أن هذا الذي ذكرناه محتمل وجوه : الأول : أن العرض عندكم في الوقت الثاني مستحيل البقاء، وإذا كان كذلك فوجوده في الوقت الأول جائز الوجود، ثم إن عدمه في الوقت الثاني واجب لذاته، فإذا عقل أن يكون عدمه بعد وجوده عدما واجبا لذاته بحيث يستحيل عقلا أن لا يصير معدوما فلم لا يجوز أن يصير وجوده بعد عدمه واجبا لذاته، بحيث يستحيل عقلا أن لا يصير موجوداً؟ والثاني: وهو أن هذا الآن الذي هو آخر الماضي وأول المستقبل كما وجد يمتنع بقاؤه ؛ لأن الحاضر لا يعقل أن يكون هو عين المستقبل، فإذن هذا الآن واجب التجدد حين حدث، واجب الفناء بعد ذلك، وإذا عقل هذا في الآنات التي هي أجزاء الزمان فلم لا يعقل مثله في جميع الحوادث ؟ والثالث : العالم بشرط كونه مسبوقا بالعدم سبقا زمانيا يمتنع حصوله في الأزل ؛ لأن الجمع بين النقيضين محال، ثم في ما لا يزال حدث ذلك الإمكان وحدوث ذلك الإمكان ليس بالفاعل ؛ لأن كل ما كان حصوله بالفاعل كان ممتنع الحصول عند فرض عدم الفاعل وصحة وجود العالم في لا يزال حاصلة سواء حصل الفاعل أو لم يحصل، فعلمنا أنه حدثت هذه الصحة لا بالفاعل بل لذاته، فثبت أن حدوث الشيء لذاته معقول.
For it might be said: Why is it not permissible to say that [a thing] was non-existent and then at that particular time turned into something necessary in itself? And that which indicates that what we have mentioned is possible are aspects: First: An accident, according to you, is impossible of persistence in the second moment. If so, its existence in the first moment is contingent, and then its non-existence in the second moment is necessary in itself. So if it is conceivable that its non-existence after its existence is a non-existence that is necessary in itself, such that it is intellectually impossible for it not to become non-existent, why is it not permissible that its existence after its non-existence becomes necessary in itself, such that it is intellectually impossible for it not to become existent? Second: This 'now' (al-an) which is the end of the past and the beginning of the future, once it exists, its persistence is impossible because the present cannot rationally be identical with the future. Thus this 'now' is necessarily renewing when it occurs and necessarily perishing after that. If this is conceivable for the 'now's which are parts of time, why is the like not conceivable for all temporal events? Third: The world, conditioned by being preceded by non-existence temporally, is impossible to occur in pre-eternity (al-azal) because the conjunction of two opposites is impossible. Then, in the future (ma la yazal), that possibility (al-imkan) occurs, and the occurrence of that possibility is not by an agent (bi-l-fa'il) because everything whose occurrence is by an agent would be impossible to occur when the agent is assumed non-existent, yet the possibility of the world's existence in the future is present regardless of whether the agent exists or not. Thus we know that this possibility occurred not by an agent but by itself. So it is established that the temporal occurrence of a thing by itself is conceivable.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage presents an objection to the argument that every temporal thing is contingent and needs a temporizer. The objector suggests that a thing might become necessary in itself at a particular time without requiring an external agent. Three arguments are given. First, regarding accidents: an accident is impossible to persist into the second moment; its existence in the first moment is contingent, but its non-existence in the second moment is necessary in itself. So if non-existence can be necessary in itself by simply following existence, why cannot existence become necessary in itself after non-existence? Second, regarding the 'now' (the present instant): each 'now' is necessarily non-persistent; it is necessarily renewed and then perishes. This necessity is based on its own nature, not an external agent. If this is accepted for instants, it should be accepted for all temporal events. Third, regarding the world: the world, being preceded by non-existence, could not exist in pre-eternity. Then at some future time, its possibility arises. This possibility arises not by an agent but by itself, because the possibility is present whether or not the agent exists. Therefore, temporal occurrence by itself (without an agent) is conceivable. These three points challenge the necessity of an external temporizer for temporal things.

Key Terms
الجائز (al-ja'iz)
الواجب (al-wajib)
الآن (al-an)
الإمكان (al-imkan)
الفاعل (al-fa'il)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the objector is denying the need for a Creator entirely.
Correction: The objection is specifically about whether a thing can become necessary in itself, not a denial of a Creator. It challenges the reasoning, not the conclusion.

Checkpoint
In the first point of the objection, what example does the objector use to argue that non-existence can be necessary in itself?
A) The non-existence of the world in pre-eternity
B) The non-existence of an accident in the second moment
C) The non-existence of the 'now'
D) The non-existence of a substance

Key Takeaway
Razi's objection demonstrates that the contingency argument must address the possibility of a thing becoming necessary in itself without an external agent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If it is conceivable that a thing becomes necessary by itself, does that shake your certainty that you need God? Examine your heart: have you ever thought you could be self-sufficient without Him? What would it take for you to truly rely on Allah alone?
?
The objector argues that non-existence can become necessary in itself—but your own existence is contingent. Are you living as if your existence is necessary, forgetting that you could cease to be at any moment? How would your daily priorities change if you truly felt your contingency before Allah?
?
The third objection says possibility occurs by itself—but do you act as if your choices and abilities come from yourself alone, ignoring that your very capacity to choose is given by Allah? How can you remind yourself hourly that you are not self-sufficient and that every breath is a gift?

Second Objection: The Impossibility of Contingent Existence
Razi records an objection that the concept of a contingent existent is impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's second objection to the contingency argument, which argues that the very concept of a possible existent is logically incoherent, and grasp the two main cases considered.

Core Idea
The concept of possibility may be self-contradictory if existence and essence are identical.

Razi presents a powerful objection that strikes at the heart of the contingency argument. He concedes that temporals are possible, but then asks: is the notion of a possible existent even coherent? By analyzing the relationship between existence and essence, he attempts to show that possibility leads to absurdity. This lesson unpacks the first part of that objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation, to get the overall flow of the objection.
2
Identify the two main cases
The objector considers two cases: (1) existence is identical to essence, (2) existence is different from essence. Note the structure of the argument.
3
Understand the first case argument
If existence is identical to essence, then possibility requires the essence to be both existent and non-existent at the same time, which is impossible.
This shows the contradiction in the concept of possibility under the identity view.
4
Follow the second case sub-options
If existence is different from essence, the subject of possibility could be essence, existence, or the connection. The objector argues each leads to absurdity. Only the first sub-option (essence as subject) is fully explained; the text breaks off.
The first sub-option: saying 'blackness can be blackness' implies judging blackness as non-blackness.
5
Reflect on the radical nature
This objection does not just challenge the conclusion of the contingency argument but its very foundation—the coherence of possibility itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلمنا أن ما ذكرتم يدل على أن كل محدث ممكن لذاته لكنه معارض بحجج دالة على أن القول بوجود شيء ممكن الوجود محال الحجة الأولى: أنا إما أن نقول : الوجود نفس الماهية وإما أن نقول: الوجود غير الماهية وعلى كل واحد من القولين فالقول بالإمكان والجواز غير معقول، أما على القول بأن الوجود نفس الماهية فنقول : الإمكان معقول؛ وذلك لأن الشيء الموصوف بالإمكان هو الذي يجوز أن يكون موجودا ويجوز أن يكون معدوما، والحكم بجواز الوجود والعدم على تلك الماهية يستدعي كون الماهية مقارنة للوجود، ومقارنة للعدم ومتى كان الأمر كذلك امتنع أن يقال : الوجود هو نفس الماهية، وأما على القول بأن الوجود غير الماهية فلأن على هذا التقدير إما أن يكون الموصوف بالإمكان هو الماهية أو الوجود أو كون الماهية موصوفة بالوجود، والكل محال، وإما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الموصوف بالإمكان والماهية؛ لأن كل ما كان ممكن الثبوت لم يمتنع تقرره مع عدم ذلك الشيء، فلو قلنا : السواد يمكن أن يكون سوادا كأن معناه السواد يمكن أن يحكم عليه بأنه سواد ومعلوم أن ذلك محال ؛ لأنه يقتضي أن يكون حال كونه سوادا محكوما عليه بأنه غير سواد، وذلك محال، وإما أنه لا يجوز أن يكون الموصوف بالإمكان هو الوجود؛ لأنه يرجع حاصله إلى أن الوجود
We grant that what you mentioned indicates that every temporal is possible in itself, but it is opposed by proofs indicating that the statement that something possible of existence exists is impossible. The first proof: either we say that existence is identical to the essence (nafs al-mahiyya), or we say that existence is different from the essence. On each of the two statements, the statement of possibility (imkan) and contingency (jawaz) is inconceivable. As for the statement that existence is identical to the essence, we say: possibility is conceivable? Because the thing described by possibility is that which may exist and may not exist, and the judgment of the possibility of existence and non-existence upon that essence requires that the essence be concomitant with existence and concomitant with non-existence. And when this is the case, it is impossible to say that existence is identical to the essence. As for the statement that existence is different from the essence, then on this supposition, either the thing described by possibility is the essence, or existence, or the fact that the essence is described by existence. All are impossible. And it is not permissible that the thing described by possibility be the essence, because everything whose attribution is possible is not prevented from being established together with the absence of that thing. If we say 'blackness can be blackness,' it is as if the meaning is that blackness can be judged to be blackness, and it is known that this is impossible because it would require that while being blackness, it is judged as non-blackness, which is impossible. And it is not permissible that the thing described by possibility be existence, because its conclusion returns to that existence...
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage presents a second objection to the contingency argument. The objector concedes that the previous argument shows temporals are possible, but raises a new difficulty: the very concept of a possible existent is incoherent. Two cases are considered. First, if existence is identical to essence, then the essence would need to be both existent and non-existent simultaneously to be possible, which is impossible. Second, if existence is different from essence, then the subject of possibility could be the essence itself, existence, or the connection between them. The objector argues each option leads to absurdity. The explanation for the first option (essence as subject) is given: if blackness is said to be possible as blackness, it would require judging blackness as non-blackness while it is blackness, which is contradictory. The argument continues, but the text is cut off before completing the discussion of the second option (existence as subject).

Key Terms
الوجود (al-wujud)
الماهية (al-mahiyya)
الإمكان (al-imkan)
الجواز (al-jawaz)
الموصوف بالإمكان (al-mawsuf bi-l-imkan)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This objection is rejecting the need for a Creator.
Correction: This objection attacks the foundation of contingency itself; it is not rejecting the need for a Creator but questioning whether the concept of possibility is coherent.

Checkpoint
In the first case, assuming existence is identical to essence, why does the objector say possibility is inconceivable?
A) Because the essence would have to be both existent and non-existent simultaneously
B) Because the essence is eternal
C) Because existence is separate from essence
D) Because the essence cannot be described

Key Takeaway
Razi's second objection challenges the coherence of possibility itself, not just the inference to a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever casually described yourself or something around you as 'merely possible' without considering the profound metaphysical implications? How does this objection force you to reexamine your own existence—are you truly possible, or does the label of possibility itself dissolve under scrutiny?
?
If the concept of possibility is incoherent, then the contingency argument for God's existence collapses. Does that shake your confidence in rational proofs for God? Or does it drive you to seek a different foundation? What would you be left with if this objection stood?
?
When you reflect on your own existence, do you sense a deep contingency—that you might not have been? This passage suggests that such a thought might be logically flawed. Does that make you feel more grounded or more unsettled? How can your relationship with the Necessary Being be strengthened even if contingency is problematic?
